Al-Razi's Defense: Familiarity and Abstraction Explain Apparent Weakness
Al-Razi rebuts the objection that the proposition is weaker than obvious truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's rebuttal to the second objection that the utility of correct reasoning is weaker than obvious self-evident truths, and why the difference in strength does not impair self-evidence.

Core Idea
Strength of self-evidence varies due to familiarity and abstraction, not due to lack of necessity.

In the second objection, critics compare the proposition 'Correct reasoning yields knowledge' to the obvious self-evident truth 'One is half of two', claiming the former is weaker because it admits the possibility of the opposite. Imam al-Razi counters by specifying two factors that account for the perceived difference: familiarity (al-ulf wa al-isti'nās) and a difference in the degree of abstraction. This lesson unpacks this rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that contains the second objection and al-Razi's response. Note the comparison made.
The objection starts with 'قولكم: التفاوت بينه وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين'.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says: The proposition in question is weaker than the statement 'One is half of two' because it admits the possibility of the opposite (l-iḥtimāl al-naqīḍ), even if remote.
The objector thinks this shows the proposition is not truly self-evident.
3
Identify al-Razi's two responses
Al-Razi denies that the difference is due to lack of self-evidence. Instead, he gives two alternative explanations: (1) familiarity with the common statement due to frequent occurrence, and (2) difference in the abstraction of the two sides.
The Arabic phrases are: 'الإلف والاستئناس' and 'تفاوت في تجريد الطرفين'.
4
Reflect on the implication
These two factors (familiarity and abstraction) are external and do not affect the intrinsic self-evidence. Thus, the objection fails.

Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Imam al-Razi and his followers who choose the view that the utility of correct reasoning is known necessarily (ḍarūrī). It defends against two objections. First, the objection that if it were necessary, it would not be disagreed upon is rebutted because disagreement does not preclude necessity; some people deny self-evident matters entirely due to obscurity in conception or difficulty in abstracting the terms from accidental attributes. Second, the objection that it is weaker than obvious self-evident truths because it admits the possibility of the opposite is rebutted by explaining that the difference in strength is due either to familiarity (al-ulf wa al-isti'nās) with the common statement or to a difference in the degree of abstraction, neither of which undermines its self-evident nature. The passage references a previous rebuttal of the deniers of self-evident matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a self-evident proposition appears weaker than another, it cannot be truly self-evident.
Correction: Perceived strength can vary due to familiarity and abstraction; the underlying necessity remains unaffected.

A) Familiarity and abstraction
B) Complexity and length
C) Possibility of opposite and logical structure
D) Social agreement and cultural factors

Key Takeaway
The apparent weakness of a seemingly self-evident proposition can be due to familiarity or abstraction, not a defect in its necessity.

Imam al-Haramayn's Defense: Correct Reasoning's Utility is Theoretical
Imam al-Haramayn defends the view that the utility of correct reasoning is theoretical and not circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Haramayn holds that the utility of correct reasoning is theoretical and why this view does not lead to a vicious circle.

Core Idea
Correct reasoning can prove its own utility without circularity.

Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and his school assert that the proposition 'correct reasoning is beneficial for knowledge' is theoretical (naẓarī), not self-evident. They also argue that proving this proposition by reasoning does not involve contradiction or circularity. This passage addresses objections from those who think such proof is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening position
Begin by reading the passage where Imam al-Haramayn states that the utility of correct reasoning is theoretical and that there is no contradiction in proving proof by proof.
Notice the phrase 'ولا تناقض في إثبات النظر بالنظر'.
2
Understand the objection of circularity
The deniers object that if correct reasoning is beneficial, then using reasoning to prove that very fact leads to circularity. Understand this challenge.
The objection is that such proof would be self-referential.
3
Examine Imam al-Haramayn's response
Imam al-Haramayn responds by clarifying that the proof concerns the general proposition, not a particular instance, and is based on necessary implication. Follow his reasoning.
The key is the concept of 'lāzim bayyin' (necessary implication).
4
Consider the classification of reasoning types
The passage mentions that some reasoning forms are necessary (like the first figure) while others are theoretical. Note how the general title 'correct reasoning' avoids the issue of particulars.
This addresses the objection that the utility cannot be both necessary and theoretical.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And a group among them Imam al-Haramayn said: It is theoretical and there is no contradiction in proving proof by proof. Commentary
This passage presents the position of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and his followers, who hold that the utility of correct reasoning is theoretical (naẓarī) and that there is no contradiction in proving proof by proof (ithbāt al-naẓar bil-naẓar). The commentary criticizes a previous answer as strained, because the objection targets the benefit itself, not the knowledge of it. A better approach is to argue that if correct reasoning were beneficial for knowledge, it would make the reasoner aware of that benefit when considering the premises and conclusion, based on a necessary implication. The passage also addresses a potential objection: that the utility of reasoning is necessary in some cases (like the first figure) and theoretical in others, so it cannot be absolutely one or the other. The response is that the discussion concerns the general title 'correct reasoning' without specifying particulars. The text repeats some points and includes further commentary on the contradiction charge.

Al-Razi's Defense of the Necessity of Correct Reasoning
Al-Razi defends necessity of reasoning against two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi defends the claim that the benefit of correct reasoning is necessary (daruri) and how he refutes objections based on disagreement and comparative strength.

Core Idea
Disagreement does not disprove necessity; perceived weakness is due to familiarity or conceptual difficulty.

Al-Razi's view that correct reasoning necessarily yields knowledge was contested by those who pointed to disagreements and to the fact that this judgment seems weaker than obvious truths. In this passage, al-Razi responds to both objections, showing that neither undermines the necessity of the judgment. By understanding his arguments, you will see how kalam thinkers defended foundational principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get the overall argument before diving into details.
Notice the structure: claim, two objections, two responses.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims disagreement disproves necessity. The second claims the judgment is weaker than obvious truths like 'the one is half of two'.
3
Analyze the first response
Al-Razi argues that disagreement can occur due to obscurity in conceptualizing the two terms, not because the judgment is not necessary. He even notes that some have denied basic intuitions.
This shows that disagreement is not a proof against necessity.
4
Analyze the second response
The perceived weakness is due to familiarity (the adage is more common) or difficulty in isolating the terms. Neither affects the intuitive nature of the judgment.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on how both responses preserve the necessity of reasoning. The objections are answered without compromising the foundational status of the judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاختار) في جواب الشبهة ( طائفة منهم الإمام الرازي أنه ضروري ) كما حققناه من كلامه في النهاية ( قولكم لو كان ضروريا لم يختلف فيه قلنا : لا نسلم بل قد يختلف فيه مع كونه ضروريا ( قوم قليل وكيف يقال : لا يجوز اختلافهم فيه ( وقد أنكر (قوم) من العقلاء ( البديهيات رأساً ) كما عرفت ) وذلك ) الاختلاف الواقع هاهنا، إنما يكون ) لخفاء في تصور (الطرفين في هذا الحكم البديهي ( ولعسر مناط في تجريدهما ) عن العوارض واللواحق ليتحصلا في الذهن على الوجه الذي هو الحكم ، فلما لم يجردوهما كما هو حقهما أنكروا الحكم بينهما، وذلك لا يقدح في كونه بديهياً (كما مر) في جواب الشبهة الرابعة لمنكري البديهيات بالكلية ( قولكم : التفاوت بينه وبين قولنا الواحد نصف (الاثنين وكونه أدنى منه في القوة إنما هو ( لاحتماله للنقيض ولو بأبعد وجه :(قلنا : ممنوع بل) ذلك التفاوت (إما للإلف والاستئناس بذلك القول لوروده على الذهن كثيرا بخلاف ما نحن فيه ( أو لتفاوت في تجريد (الطرفين ولا شك أن التفاوت الناشئ من هذين لا يقدح في البداهة
And he chose) in response to the doubt (a group among them, Imam al-Razi, that it is necessary (daruri)) as we verified from his words in al-Nihayah. (Your statement: if it were necessary, there would be no disagreement about it. We say: we do not accept. Rather, there may be disagreement about it while it is necessary, and a small group [may disagree]. And how can it be said that disagreement about it is not permissible? And a group of rational thinkers have denied basic intuitions (badihiyyat) altogether, as you know. And that disagreement occurring here is only due to obscurity in the conceptualization of the two terms (al-tarafayn) in this intuitive judgment, and due to difficulty in the criterion of isolating them from accidental attributes and adjuncts to obtain them in the mind in the manner that is the judgment. So when they did not isolate them as they ought to, they denied the judgment between them, and that does not impair it being intuitive, as passed in the response to the fourth doubt of the deniers of basic intuitions altogether. (Your statement: the difference between it and our statement 'the one is half of two' and its being lower in strength is only due to its possibility of the opposite, even in the most remote way. We say: it is denied. Rather, that difference is either due to familiarity and comfort with that statement because of its frequent occurrence in the mind, contrary to what we are discussing, or due to a difference in isolating the two terms, and there is no doubt that the difference arising from these two does not impair its being intuitive.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending al-Razi's claim that the benefit of correct reasoning (al-nazar al-sahih) is necessary (daruri). It begins by stating al-Razi's position and then addresses two objections: (1) if it were necessary, there would be no disagreement; (2) the judgment 'al-nazar yufid al-'ilm' is weaker than obvious truths like 'the one is half of two'. The response to the first objection is that disagreement does not disprove necessity, as even basic intuitions (badihiyyat) have been denied by some thinkers due to lack of proper conceptualization. The response to the second objection is that the perceived weakness is due to familiarity or difficulty in isolating the terms, not to a lack of necessity. The passage concludes that such differences do not impair basicness.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
ضروري (daruri)
بديهي (badihi)
طرفان (tarafan)
إلف (ulf)
استئناس (isti'nas)
تجريد (tajrid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a belief is necessary, everyone must agree on it.
Correction: Disagreement can arise from failure to properly conceptualize the terms, not from the judgment's lack of necessity.

Checkpoint
How does the commentary respond to the objection that if the benefit of reasoning were necessary (daruri), there would be no disagreement about it?
A) It denies that there is disagreement.
B) It argues that disagreement is impossible for necessary truths.
C) It states that disagreement can occur when the two terms are not properly conceptualized, just as basic intuitions are sometimes denied.
D) It concedes that the benefit of reasoning is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that objections to the necessity of reasoning can be answered without compromising its foundational status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth of faith simply because you found it hard to conceptualize? Al-Razi warns that obscurity in understanding the terms can lead to denial. What is one Islamic teaching you have struggled with, and what would it take for you to properly isolate its terms in your mind? Commit to one step this week to seek clarity on that teaching.
?
When you defend your beliefs, do you rely on familiar phrases and comfortable arguments, or do you genuinely examine the logical structure? This lesson shows that familiarity can make a proposition seem stronger. Is there a belief you hold that you have never critically examined? Will you take time to isolate the subject and predicate and see if the judgment holds?
?
Al-Razi argues that disagreement does not disprove necessity. However, when you encounter someone who denies an obvious Islamic truth, do you dismiss them entirely, or do you consider that they might have a conceptual difficulty? How can you help them clarify the terms without compromising the truth? Make a specific intention to approach one such disagreement with patience and clarity.

Imam al-Haramayn's Claim and the Objection of Contradiction
Opening of the debate: Imam al-Haramayn's view and al-Razi's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial claim that reasoning is theoretical and the objection that establishing reasoning by reasoning involves a contradiction.

Core Idea
Is establishing reasoning by reasoning a contradiction? The debate begins.

This lesson explores the opening of a complex debate: Imam al-Haramayn asserts that reasoning (nazar) is theoretical (nazari) and that there is no contradiction in establishing reasoning by reasoning. Deniers object, and al-Razi raises a specific problem of self-establishment leading to contradiction. We will see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read Imam al-Haramayn's view
Read the passage where Imam al-Haramayn states that reasoning is theoretical and that there is no contradiction in establishing reasoning by reasoning.
Notice the phrase 'لا تناقض' (no contradiction).
2
Understand the deniers' doubt
The deniers argue that if reasoning benefits knowledge, that very benefit creates a doubt about whether the benefit itself is known.
This is a subtle point about the knowability of the benefit.
3
Consider al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi objects that establishing reasoning by reasoning would be establishing a thing by itself, which requires prior knowledge and leads to a contradiction.
Focus on the argument about being both sought and instrument.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The passage concludes that there is no escape except by claiming that the benefit is necessary (daruri).
This sets up the response in the next lesson.

في الشكل الأول نظري في باقي الأشكال، فكيف يصح اختيار أنه ضروري مطلقاً، ما ذهب إليه الرازي أو نظري مطلقا على ما ذهب إليه إمام الحرمين وأجيب بأن الكلام فيما إذا أخذ عنوان الموضوع هو النظر الصحيح، وما ذكر من التفصيل قطعاً إنما هو في الخصوصيات وأنكر عليه الإمام الرازي) في النهاية ( فقال : إن إثبات الشيء بنفسه يقتضي أن يعلم به قبل نفسه ليمكن إثباته به ) وذلك يستلزم أن يعلم حين ما لا يعلم وتلخيصه أنه من حيث هو مطلوب يجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً حال الطلب، ومن حيث أنه آلة من الطلب يجب أن يكون حاصلا في تلك الحال ( وهو تناقض ) قال : فبطل ما توهموه أن نفي الشيء بنفسه تناقض لاجتماع نفيه وإثباته معا بخلاف إثبات الشيء بنفسه، إذ لا تناقض فيه أصلاً ، فظهر أن إثبات كل النظر بالنظر يشتمل على تناقض من نفي كل النظر بالنظر متناقض من وجه آخر فلا مخلص إلا في دعوى الضرورة كما لخصناها ( والجواب أنه ( أي إمام الحرمين ( إنما يمنع كون إثبات كون النظر بالنظر إثباتاً للشيء بنفسه لا أنه يسلم ذلك ويمنع كونه تناقضاً) حتى يتجه عليه ذلك الإنكار ) وتحقيقه ) أي تحقيق ما ذكرناه من أن إثبات النظر بالنظر ليس إثباتا للشيء بنفسه وإن أوهمته العبارة ( أنا نثبت القضية الكلية القائلة كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه ما ينافي العلم فإنه يفيده ) أو المهملة ) القائلة : النظر قد يفيد العلم ( على اختلاف التحريرين بمشخصة ) أي بقضية شخصية حكم فيها بقوله لاستلزامه كون الشيء معلوماً حين ما ليس معلوماً، وإن ما نقله عن الإمام إعادة لذلك فالصواب أن يقال : في شرح قوله تناقض كنفي الشيء بنفسه، ثم يحرر كلام إمام الحرمين بأنه لا تناقض في إثبات الشيء بنفسه لأنه إنما يقتضي ثبوت الشيء فقط بخلاف نفيه بنفسه، فإنه يستلزم انتفاء الشيء وثبوته معاً، وإنه تناقض ثم يورد عليه إنكار الإمام بأنه وإن لم يكن في إثبات الشي بنفسه التناقض الذي في نفي الشيء بنفسه، إلا أنه يستلزم تناقضاً آخر وهو أن يكون الشيء معلوماً، وأن لا يكون معلوماً في حالة واحدة . قوله: (وتلخيصه أنه إلخ) الحيثيات للتعليل لا للتقييد فلا يرد منع التناقض لاختلاف الحيثيتين. قوله : ( على تناقض من وجه) وهو أن يكون معلوماً وأن لا يكون معلوماً في حالة واحدة . قوله : ( من وجه آخر وهو أن يكون النظر ثابتاً ومنتفياً . قوله : ( وإن أوهمته العبارة ) أعني قولنا إثبات النظر بالنظر . قوله : (أي بقضية شخصية أي وهي أن هذا النظر مفيد للعلم فإن قيل : إثبات الكلية والمهملة إذا كانت بنظر مخصوص كان الإثبات بنفس ذلك النظر الجزئي لأنه مفيد للعلم، قلت : إثباتها بذلك النظر متوقف على صحة مقدماته واستلزامه لها وهو معنى الإفادة، فيكون إثباتها موقوفاً على قولنا هذا النظر مفيد للعلم. قوله: (وتلخيصه أنه من حيث هو مطلوب إلخ ) الحيثيتان المذكورتان للتعليل لا للتقييد فلا ينافيان التناقض .
And a group among them, Imam al-Haramayn, said: It is theoretical (nazari) and there is no contradiction in establishing reasoning by reasoning. This response is forced because the title of the inquiry... then the deniers said: Because reasoning is beneficial for knowledge indicates that the doubt is for deniers of the benefit itself. It was said: It is better to say: the intended meaning of the evidences that yield the negation of knowability is that if it yielded knowledge, it would yield that it is knowledge when observing the two terms, based on its being a necessary implication (lazim bayyin) even in the broader sense, and the negation of the implication indicates the negation of the implied. And you are aware that the discussion is about the evidences that yield negation of the knowability of this proposition, not about that what reasoning yielded is knowledge, for this is another doubt for the negators, as his statement: (that it is necessary (daruri) i.e., under the title of correct reasoning, even if the individuals of its subject matter in themselves are some necessary like the first figure and the exceptional syllogism, and some theoretical like the remaining figures. So it is not valid that the choice that it is absolutely necessary, or that it is theoretical, is incorrect due to its division into the two. His statement will come: (and there is no contradiction in establishing reasoning by reasoning. It is not hidden that there is no ground for preventing contradiction after it has been established that correct reasoning is beneficial for knowledge, indicating that the doubt is for deniers of the benefit itself. So it is better to say: the evidences that yield negation of knowability is that if it yielded knowledge, it would yield that it is knowledge when observing the two terms, based on its being a necessary implication even in the broader sense, and the negation of the implication indicates the negation of the implied. His statement: (among them Imam al-Razi that it is necessary (daruri). It was said: against him, it is not hidden that the fact that reasoning is beneficial for knowledge is necessary in the first figure, theoretical in the remaining figures. So how is it correct to choose that it is absolutely necessary, as al-Razi held, or absolutely theoretical, as Imam al-Haramayn held? And it was answered: the discussion is when the title of the subject is correct reasoning, and what was mentioned of detail is definitely only in the particularities. And Imam al-Razi objected to him in al-Nihayah, saying: indeed establishing a thing by itself requires that it be known before itself to be able to establish it by it. And that necessitates that it be known at a time when it is not known, and its summary: from the perspective that it is sought, it must not be present at the time of seeking, and from the perspective that it is an instrument for seeking, it must be present at that time, and it is a contradiction. He said: thus what they imagined that negating a thing by itself is a contradiction because of the combination of its negation and affirmation together, unlike establishing a thing by itself, for there is no contradiction in it at all, is invalid. So it became apparent that establishing all reasoning by reasoning includes a contradiction, and negating all reasoning by reasoning is contradictory from another aspect. So there is no escape except in claiming necessity (darura) as we summarized. And the answer: that he (i.e., Imam al-Haramayn) only prevents that establishing reasoning by reasoning is an establishment of a thing by itself, not that he concedes that and prevents it being a contradiction, so that objection would be directed at him. And its verification: i.e., verification of what we mentioned, that establishing reasoning by reasoning is not an establishment of a thing by itself, even if the expression suggests it: we establish the universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) stating: every correct reasoning in certainties that is not followed by what contradicts knowledge, indeed it yields knowledge, or the particular (al-muhmala) stating: reasoning may yield knowledge, according to the difference of the two formulations, by a particular proposition (qadiyya shakhsiyya) in which it is judged by his statement due to its requiring that the thing be known when it is not known. And what he narrated from the Imam is a repetition of that. So the correct thing is to say in the explanation of his statement: contradiction is like negating a thing by itself. Then let the statement of Imam al-Haramayn be formulated: there is no contradiction in establishing a thing by itself, because it only requires the existence of the thing only, unlike negating it by itself, for it requires the negation and existence of the thing together, and that is a contradiction. Then the Imam's objection is raised against him: even if there is not in establishing a thing by itself the contradiction that is in negating a thing by itself, it requires another contradiction: that the thing be known and not be known in one state. His statement: (and its summary: that it is etc.) the two aspects (al-haythiyyat) are for explanation, not for restriction, so the prevention of contradiction due to difference of the two aspects is not valid. His statement: (on a contradiction from one aspect) which is that it be known and not be known in one state. His statement: (from another aspect which is that reasoning be established and negated. His statement: (and if the expression suggests it) i.e., our statement 'establishing reasoning by reasoning'. His statement: (i.e., by a particular proposition i.e., that this reasoning is beneficial for knowledge. For if it is said: establishing the universal and the particular, if it is by a specific reasoning, then the establishment is by that particular reasoning itself because it is beneficial for knowledge, I say: its establishment by that reasoning depends on the correctness of its premises and its implication of them, and that is the meaning of benefit. So its establishment is dependent on our statement: this reasoning is beneficial for knowledge. His statement: (and its summary: that from the perspective that it is sought etc.) the two mentioned aspects are for explanation, not for restriction, so they do not contradict the contradiction.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a complex commentary refuting the claim that establishing reasoning by reasoning (ithbat al-nazar bi al-nazar) involves a contradiction. It presents Imam al-Haramayn's view that the benefit of reasoning is theoretical (nazari) and that there is no contradiction. It then details al-Razi's objection that establishing reasoning by reasoning is like establishing a thing by itself, which requires prior knowledge and creates a contradiction (being both sought and instrument). The response argues that establishment is not of the same thing but through a universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) about reasoning, not the particular reasoning itself. The commentary explains that the universal proposition is established by a particular reasoning, but that does not create a circle because the universal is not established by itself. It also clarifies that the two aspects (being sought and being an instrument) are for explanation, not restriction, so they do not negate the contradiction from a single perspective. The passage concludes with detailed philological notes on the wording.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
نظري (nazari)
تناقض (tanaqud)
لازم بين (lazim bayyin)
ضروري (daruri)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that establishing reasoning by reasoning is always a circular contradiction with no resolution.
Correction: This passage shows that the contradiction is not accepted as final; rather, it is debated, and al-Razi's objection forces a consideration of necessity as a possible solution.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why is establishing reasoning by reasoning problematic?
A) Because it leads to the conclusion that reasoning is unnecessary.
B) Because it requires reasoning to be known before itself, which is a contradiction.
C) Because it makes reasoning dependent on itself, causing infinite regress.
D) Because it contradicts the definition of reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The debate on establishing reasoning by reasoning begins with disagreement over its nature (theoretical vs. necessary) and raises the problem of self-establishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own reasoning to prove something, do you ever examine whether that reasoning itself is sound without falling into circularity? This lesson shows even scholars debate the foundation—how confident are you in your own intellectual foundations?
?
If the very instrument you use to seek truth (reason) might be contradictory when applied to itself, where does that leave your certainty? Have you ever faced a belief that seemed rationally necessary (daruri) but could not be proven without contradiction?
?
Al-Razi ended by admitting that only claiming necessity (darura) escapes the contradiction. Is there an area in your faith where you must humbly accept something as necessary without rational proof, simply because the alternative is an infinite regress or contradiction? What stops you from doing so?

Rational Judgments
advanced
8 min
Resolving the Contradiction: The Universal Proposition Defense
Response to al-Razi: distinguishing universal from particular and explaining the two aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contradiction in establishing reasoning by reasoning is resolved by distinguishing between universal and particular propositions.

Core Idea
We don't establish reasoning by itself; we establish a universal proposition about reasoning.

This lesson presents the response to al-Razi's objection. The key is that we do not establish a particular reasoning by itself, but rather a universal proposition (e.g., 'every correct reasoning yields knowledge'). This avoids circularity. The lesson also clarifies the role of the two aspects (being sought and being instrument) as explanatory, not restrictive.

Guided Path
1
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's response
Imam al-Haramayn prevents the claim that establishing reasoning by reasoning is self-establishment; he argues that it is not the same thing.
The phrase 'إنما يمنع كون إثبات كون النظر بالنظر إثباتاً للشيء بنفسه' indicates the prevention.
2
See the verification
The verification shows that we establish a universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) about reasoning, not the particular reasoning itself.
Note the distinction between universal and particular propositions.
3
Learn the distinction
The universal proposition is established by a particular reasoning, but that does not create a circle because the particular reasoning is not the same as the universal content.
This is the core of the resolution.
4
Understand the haythiyya clarification
The two aspects (being sought and being instrument) are for explanation (ta'lil), not for restriction (taqyid), so they do not negate the contradiction from a single perspective.
This addresses al-Razi's objection about the two aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : طائفة منهم إمام الحرمين : إنه نظري ولا تناقض في إثبات النظر بالنظر، هذا الجواب تعسفاً لأن عنوان البحث ثم قال المنكرون : لكون النظر مفيداً للعلم يدل على أن الشبهة لمنكري نفس الإفادة، قيل : الأولى أن يقال : المقصود من الأدلة التي تفيد نفي المعلومية أنه لو أفاد العلم أفاد كونه علماً عند ملاحظة الطرفين، بناء على أنه لازم بين ولو بالمعنى الأعم، وانتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في الأدلة التي تفيد في نفي معلومية هذه القضية لا في أن ما أفاده النظر علم، فإن هذه شبهة أخرى للنافين كما قوله : ( أنه ضروري أي بعنوان النظر الصحيح، وإن كانت أفراد موضوعها بالنظر إلى أنفسها بعضها ضرورياً كالشكل الأول، والقياس الاستثنائي وبعضها نظرياً كباقي الأشكال، فلا يرد أن اختيار كونه ضرورياً مطلقاً، أو كونه نظرياً غير صحيح لانقسامه إليهما . سيجي قوله : ( ولا تناقض في إثبات النظر بالنظر لا يخفى أنه لا وجه لمنع التناقض بعد ما أثبته النظر الصحيح مفيداً للعلم يدل على أن الشبهة لمنكري نفس الإفادة، فالأولى أن يقال: الأدلة التي تفيد نفي المعلومية هو أنه لو أفاد العلم أفاد كونها علماً عند ملاحظة الطرفين بناء على أنه لازم بين ولو بالمعنى الأعم وانتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم . قوله: (منهم الإمام الرازي أنه ضروري قيل : عليه لا خفاء في أن كون النظر مفيداً للعلم ضروري من حيث هو مطلوب يجب أن لا يكون حاصلاً حال الطلب، ومن حيث أنه آلة من الطلب يجب أن يكون حاصلا في تلك الحال ( وهو تناقض ) قال : فبطل ما توهموه أن نفي الشيء بنفسه تناقض لاجتماع نفيه وإثباته معا بخلاف His statement: (and if the expression suggests it) i.e., our statement 'establishing reasoning by reasoning'. His statement: (i.e., by a particular proposition i.e., that this reasoning is beneficial for knowledge. For if it is said: establishing the universal and the particular, if it is by a specific reasoning, then the establishment is by that particular reasoning itself because it is beneficial for knowledge, I say: its establishment by that reasoning depends on the correctness of its premises and its implication of them, and that is the meaning of benefit. So its establishment is dependent on our statement: this reasoning is beneficial for knowledge. His statement: (and its summary: that from the perspective that it is sought etc.) the two mentioned aspects are for explanation, not for restriction, so they do not contradict the contradiction.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a complex commentary refuting the claim that establishing reasoning by reasoning (ithbat al-nazar bi al-nazar) involves a contradiction. It presents Imam al-Haramayn's view that the benefit of reasoning is theoretical (nazari) and that there is no contradiction. It then details al-Razi's objection that establishing reasoning by reasoning is like establishing a thing by itself, which requires prior knowledge and creates a contradiction (being both sought and instrument). The response argues that establishment is not of the same thing but through a universal proposition (al-qadiyya al-kulliyya) about reasoning, not the particular reasoning itself. The commentary explains that the universal proposition is established by a particular reasoning, but that does not create a circle because the universal is not established by itself. It also clarifies that the two aspects (being sought and being an instrument) are for explanation, not restriction, so they do not negate the contradiction from a single perspective. The passage concludes with detailed philological notes on the wording.

Key Terms
قضية كلية (qadiyya kulliyya)
قضية شخصية (qadiyya shakhsiyya)
حيثية (haythiyya)
لازم بين (lazim bayyin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that even after the response, establishing reasoning by reasoning is still contradictory because it involves the same particular reasoning establishing itself.
Correction: The response clarifies that the establishment is of a universal proposition, not the particular reasoning itself. Thus, it avoids self-establishment.

Checkpoint
What kind of proposition is established in the response to al-Razi's objection?
A) A particular proposition about a specific reasoning.
B) A universal proposition about reasoning in general.
C) A proposition about the necessity of reasoning.
D) A proposition negating the benefit of reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The resolution lies in establishing a universal proposition about reasoning, not the particular reasoning itself, and the two aspects are explanatory, not restrictive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about your faith, do you ever fear that your own reasoning is trapped in a circle? How does the distinction between a universal principle and a particular instance free you from that fear—and what step will you take today to trust that distinction in a matter you've been avoiding?
?
The passage says the two aspects are explanatory, not restrictive. Is there an area of your belief where you treat a 'however' or 'from one perspective' as an excuse to avoid a hard truth? How would you respond if you truly accepted that perspective explains rather than restricts?
?
This lesson shows that establishing the validity of reasoning requires a universal proposition that is itself established by a particular instance. In your own spiritual life, what universal truth about God or guidance have you accepted on the basis of a particular experience or proof? Write down one such universal and the particular that grounds it—and then commit to reviewing it this week.

Proof That Sound Syllogistic Reasoning Yields Certain Knowledge
Al-Jurjani proves that sound syllogistic reasoning yields certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani proves that sound syllogistic reasoning necessarily yields certain knowledge, using self-evident premises.

Core Idea
Sound syllogistic reasoning yields certain knowledge because its premises and entailment are self-evident.

Al-Jurjani presents a concise proof that when we have a sound syllogism with true premises and valid form, the conclusion is necessarily certain. He breaks the argument into two premises that are self-evident, showing that the foundation of knowledge in theoretical matters rests on the very structure of valid reasoning. This passage is a classic example of how Islamic logicians established the epistemic value of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the author gives two slightly different formulations of the same proof.
The first formulation uses 'matter and form' language; the second uses 'obstacle' (مانع) language.
2
Identify the minor and major premises
In the first formulation, find where the author states the minor premise (الصغرى) and the major premise (الكبرى). Understand how each supports the conclusion that every sound syllogism yields certain knowledge.
3
Analyze the second formulation
Read the second expression starting from 'وقد يقال بعبارة أخرى'. Notice how it rephrases the same argument using the concepts of 'indication' (جهة الدلالة) and 'absence of obstacle' (ارتفاع المانع). Reflect on why the author repeats the argument in a different way.
The second formulation highlights that sound reasoning requires both a positive condition (indication) and removal of negatives (obstacles).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطعاً، على جزئي معين من أفراد النظر، فنقول : النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي معلوم الصحة مادة وصورة لازمة لزوماً قطعياً لما قطعاً، هو حق وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حق فالنتيجة في كل قياس صحيح حقة قطعاً، وهذا معنى قولنا كل نظري قطعي المادة والصورة مفيد للعلم، أما الصغرى فإذ لا معنى للعلم بصحة المادة والصورة إلا القطع بحقية المقدمات وحقية استلزامها للنتيجة، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها، وقد يقال بعبارة أخرى : هكذا كل نظر صحيح في القطعيات لا يعقبه مناف للعلم يشتمل على ما يقتضي العلم مع عدم المانع وكل ما هو مشتمل على مقتضى العلم مع انتفاء المانع يفيد العلم ويستلزمه أما الصغرى فلأن النظر الصحيح ما ينطوي على جهة الدلالة أعني العلاقة العقلية الموجبة للانتقال إلى المطلوب، وقد اعتبرنا معه ارتفاع المانع، وأما الكبرى فلامتناع تخلف الشيء عن المقتضى مع ارتفاع المانع، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان إذا نظرنا فيهما أفاد لنا العلم بأن كل نظر صحيح يفيد العلم
Decisively, concerning a specific particular individual of reasoning, we say: the result in every syllogistic reasoning whose matter and form are known to be sound is necessarily entailed by what is necessarily true; it is true and everything that is like that is true, so the result in every sound syllogism is necessarily true. And this is the meaning of our saying: every theoretical [matter] that is definitive in matter and form yields knowledge. As for the minor [premise]: because there is no meaning to knowledge of the soundness of matter and form except certainty of the truth of the premises and the truth of their entailment of the result. As for the major [premise]: it is self-evident, no doubt in it. And it may be said in another expression: thus every sound reasoning concerning necessary matters is not followed by an obstacle to knowledge; it comprises what necessitates knowledge with the absence of an obstacle, and everything that comprises the requisite of knowledge with the absence of an obstacle yields knowledge and entails it. As for the minor: because sound reasoning is that which contains an aspect of indication, i.e., the rational relationship that necessitates transition to the sought [conclusion], and we have considered with it the removal of the obstacle. As for the major: because of the impossibility of the thing lagging behind its requisite with the removal of the obstacle. In summary, here are two self-evident propositions: when we consider them they give us knowledge that every sound reasoning yields knowledge.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that sound syllogistic reasoning produces certain knowledge. The author begins by asserting decisively that for any specific instance of a particular valid reasoning, the result is necessarily true if the matter and form are sound. He then rephrases this as: every theoretical matter that is definitive in matter and form yields knowledge. He then provides two premises (a minor and a major) to justify this. The minor premise states that knowing the soundness of matter and form is nothing other than being certain of the truth of the premises and the truth of their entailment of the result. The major premise is self-evident (badihiyya), with no doubt. He then offers another expression using the concept of an 'obstacle' (man': the minor premise is that sound reasoning includes an indication that rationally necessitates the transition to the conclusion, and we have considered the removal of obstacles; the major is that it is impossible for a thing to lag behind its requisite when obstacles are removed. The author concludes that there are two self-evident propositions which, when considered, yield the knowledge that every sound reasoning produces knowledge. The proof is structured as a syllogism whose premises are self-evident.

Key Terms
نظر قياسي
مادة
صورة
بديهية
مانع
مقتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reasoning cannot produce certain knowledge; it only yields probable or likely results.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that sound syllogistic reasoning, when matter and form are sound and obstacles are removed, yields certain knowledge because the premises are self-evident and entailment is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minor premise in the second formulation of the proof?
A) The result of every sound syllogism is necessarily true.
B) Sound reasoning contains an indication that rationally necessitates the transition to the conclusion, with obstacles removed.
C) The major premise is self-evident.
D) Every theoretical matter that is definitive in matter and form yields knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Sound syllogistic reasoning produces certain knowledge because its premises are self-evident and entailment is necessary when obstacles are removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are intellectually certain of a truth in your faith through sound reasoning, do you allow emotional doubts or worldly attachments to serve as obstacles that prevent that certainty from settling in your heart? What specific obstacles are you unwilling to remove?
?
The proof shows that certainty necessarily follows when obstacles are removed. Is there an area of your life where you have sound reasoning but have not removed the obstacle of heedlessness or desire? What would it cost you to remove it today?
?
You claim to believe in Allah based on rational proofs. Yet do you live as if you are absolutely certain, or do your actions betray lingering doubt? Examine your daily priorities: do they reflect the certainty that sound reasoning demands?

The Proof as Reminder and Causal Explanation
Al-Jurjani answers the objection that proving self-evident premises contradicts their self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proving self-evident propositions does not contradict their self-evidence; it serves as a reminder or causal explanation.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths can be proven without contradiction through reminders or causal explanations.

Al-Jurjani anticipates an objection: if the two premises are self-evident, why does the author provide a proof? The commentator clarifies that such a proof is not a demonstration of something unknown but a reminder or a causal explanation (ta'lil limmi). This is a key distinction in traditional logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection: that proving self-evident premises contradicts their self-evidence.
The objection is implied in 'الاعتراض بأن الاستدلال على الصغرى والكبرى ينافي دعوى بداهتهما'.
2
Understand the answer: reminder or causal explanation
Al-Jurjani answers that the proof is either a reminder (تنبيه) or a causal explanation (تعليم لمي). Think about what these mean.
3
Reflect on self-evidence and theory
He adds that the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its cause. This shows that self-evident does not mean unprovable.
This distinction solves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أما الصغرى إلخ) استدلال على حقيتها بأنها بديهية لأن تصور طرفيها كاف في الحكم، وكل بديهي فهو حق، وكذا قوله وأما الكبرى إلخ، وزاد قوله لا شبهة فيها إشارة إلى أنها بديهية لا خفاء فيها أصلاً باعتبار الحكم، ولا باعتبار الطرفين بخلاف الصغرى، فإن فيها خفاء باعتبار الطرفين وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن الاعتراض بأن الاستدلال على الصغرى والكبرى ينافي دعوى بداهتهما المستفادة من قوله، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان إلخ والجواب بأن الاستدلال المذكور تنبيه أو تعليل لمي، والبديهي قد يكون نظرياً من حيث لميته كلام منشؤه عدم التدبر فتدبر. قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) مجمل الجواب أن هاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان بأي عبارة عبرنا بهما، إذا رتبناهما ترتيباً مخصوصاً يفيد ذلك الترتيب العلم بتلك القضية الكلية أو المهملة، فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه. قوله : ( فنقول النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي إلخ) فإن قلت : معنى قولنا النظر يفيد العلم أنه يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة فمن ينكر استلزام النظر الصحيح للنتيجة كيف يسلم لزوم النتيجة؟ قلت : المنكر هو استلزام النظر للعلم بالنتيجة والمذكور هو استلزام المقدمات للنتيجة، والفرق ظاهر وبالجملة عنوان العلمية يلاحظ هاهنا في اللازم ولا عنوان النظر فقط في جانب الملزوم فلا إشكال فتأمل . قوله : ( وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان (بدیهیتان قيل دعوى بداهتهما ينافي الصغرى والكبرى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: ما ذكر تنبيه فإن قلت قوله في التحرير الأول، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها يدل على نظرية الصغرى قلت بل أراد به الكبرى بديهية لا تحتاج إلى التنبيه كما دل عليه قوله : لا شبهة فيها، ويمكن أن يقال أيضاً البديهي قد يكون نظرياً نظراً إلى لميته كما صرح به في شرح المقاصد .
His statement: (As for the minor, etc.) is a proof of its truth by its being self-evident, because conceiving its two extremes is sufficient for judgment, and every self-evident is true. And similarly his statement: 'As for the major, etc.' And he added his statement: 'no doubt in it' as an indication that it is self-evident, with no obscurity at all in terms of judgment, nor in terms of the two extremes, unlike the minor, for it has obscurity in terms of the two extremes. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the objection that proving the minor and the major contradicts the claim of their self-evidence derived from his statement 'In summary, here are two propositions, etc.' and the answer is that the mentioned proof is a reminder or a causal explanation (limmi), and the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its cause; this speech originates from lack of reflection, so reflect. His statement: (In summary, etc.) is a summary answer that here there are two self-evident propositions, in whatever expression we express them, when we arrange them in a particular order that order yields knowledge of that universal or indefinite [general] proposition, so it is not proving a thing by itself. His statement: (We say: the result in every syllogistic reasoning, etc.) For if you say: the meaning of our saying 'reasoning yields knowledge' is that it entails knowledge of the result; so how can one who denies that sound reasoning entails the result accept the entailment of the result? I say: the denier is of the entailment of reasoning for knowledge of the result, while what is mentioned is the entailment of the premises for the result, and the difference is clear. In summary, the aspect of knowledge is observed here in the necessary [consequent], and not the aspect of reasoning only in the side of the necessitating [antecedent], so no problem. Reflect. His statement: (In summary, here are two self-evident propositions.) It was said: claiming their self-evidence contradicts the minor and major [premises that are proven]. Unless it is said: what was mentioned is a reminder. For if you say: his statement in the first exposition 'As for the major, it is self-evident, no doubt in it' indicates the theoretical nature of the minor. I say: rather he meant that the major is self-evident and does not need a reminder, as indicated by his statement 'no doubt in it'. And it is also possible to say: the self-evident may be theoretical with respect to its cause, as is stated in Sharh al-Maqasid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the two self-evident propositions against objections. The commentator first explains that the proof of the minor and major premises does not contradict their self-evidence; rather, it is a reminder (tanbih) or a causal explanation (ta'lil limmi). He notes that the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its cause. He then clarifies that the ordering of the two self-evident propositions yields knowledge of the universal or general proposition without circularity. Next, he addresses an objection: if someone says that the meaning of 'reasoning yields knowledge' is that it entails knowledge of the result, then how can one who denies that sound reasoning entails the result accept the entailment of the result? The answer distinguishes between the entailment of the premises for the result (which is conceded) and the entailment of reasoning for knowledge of the result (which is denied). Finally, he responds to another objection: claiming the two propositions are self-evident contradicts the minor and major premises that are proven. The reply distinguishes between the first exposition where the major is said to be self-evident (no need for reminder) and the minor which may need a reminder. The self-evident can also be theoretical with respect to its cause, as in Sharh al-Maqasid.

Key Terms
بديهية
تنبيه
تعليم لمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The self-evident cannot be proven; any attempt to prove it contradicts its self-evidence.
Correction: Demonstrating self-evident propositions does not contradict their self-evidence; it serves as a reminder or causal explanation.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jurjani, what is the proof of the two premises called?
A) A demonstration (burhan)
B) A reminder (tanbih) or causal explanation (ta'lil limmi)
C) A refutation (radd)
D) A doubt (shubha)

Key Takeaway
Proving self-evident propositions does not negate their self-evidence; it serves as a reminder or causal explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you demanded a proof for something you already knew to be true in your heart, and what was the real reason for your doubt?
?
Is your hesitation to accept certain truths of faith due to a lack of reflection on their causes, or are you avoiding the self-evident because it demands change? What will you do today to reflect deeply?
?
Do you treat the truths of Islam as merely theoretical or as self-evident judgments that require no proof for action? Which area of your life are you still waiting for proof before committing?

Avoiding Circularity in Self-Evident Premises
Al-Jurjani explains that the ordering of the two self-evident propositions yields knowledge without circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the arrangement of two self-evident propositions yields knowledge of a universal proposition without circularity.

Core Idea
Arranging self-evident propositions in a specific order yields knowledge without self-proof.

After addressing the objection about proving self-evident propositions, Al-Jurjani provides a concise summary answer that clarifies the structure of the argument. He explains that there are two self-evident propositions; when arranged in a particular syllogistic order, they produce knowledge of a universal or indefinite proposition. This is not circular (proving a thing by itself).

Guided Path
1
Read the summary answer
Read the passage starting with 'وبالجملة إلخ'. Understand that there are two self-evident propositions.
These are the minor and major premises.
2
Analyze the arrangement
The propositions are arranged in a specific order (syllogistic form), which yields knowledge of a universal or indefinite proposition.
The arrangement is key, not the content alone.
3
See why no circularity
The conclusion is not the same as any premise; it emerges from the arrangement. Hence, it is not 'proving a thing by itself'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أما الصغرى إلخ) استدلال على حقيتها بأنها بديهية لأن تصور طرفيها كاف في الحكم، وكل بديهي فهو حق، وكذا قوله وأما الكبرى إلخ، وزاد قوله لا شبهة فيها إشارة إلى أنها بديهية لا خفاء فيها أصلاً باعتبار الحكم، ولا باعتبار الطرفين بخلاف الصغرى، فإن فيها خفاء باعتبار الطرفين وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن الاعتراض بأن الاستدلال على الصغرى والكبرى ينافي دعوى بداهتهما المستفادة من قوله، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان إلخ والجواب بأن الاستدلال المذكور تنبيه أو تعليل لمي، والبديهي قد يكون نظرياً من حيث لميته كلام منشؤه عدم التدبر فتدبر. قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) مجمل الجواب أن هاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان بأي عبارة عبرنا بهما، إذا رتبناهما ترتيباً مخصوصاً يفيد ذلك الترتيب العلم بتلك القضية الكلية أو المهملة، فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه. قوله : ( فنقول النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي إلخ) فإن قلت : معنى قولنا النظر يفيد العلم أنه يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة فمن ينكر استلزام النظر الصحيح للنتيجة كيف يسلم لزوم النتيجة؟ قلت : المنكر هو استلزام النظر للعلم بالنتيجة والمذكور هو استلزام المقدمات للنتيجة، والفرق ظاهر وبالجملة عنوان العلمية يلاحظ هاهنا في اللازم ولا عنوان النظر فقط في جانب الملزوم فلا إشكال فتأمل . قوله : ( وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان (بدیهیتان قيل دعوى بداهتهما ينافي الصغرى والكبرى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: ما ذكر تنبيه فإن قلت قوله في التحرير الأول، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها يدل على نظرية الصغرى قلت بل أراد به الكبرى بديهية لا تحتاج إلى التنبيه كما دل عليه قوله : لا شبهة فيها، ويمكن أن يقال أيضاً البديهي قد يكون نظرياً نظراً إلى لميته كما صرح به في شرح المقاصد .

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two self-evident propositions are proven by themselves, leading to circular reasoning.
Correction: The arrangement of the two propositions yields new knowledge without circularity; it is not proving a thing by itself.

Checkpoint
What does the arrangement of the two self-evident propositions produce?
A) Doubt
B) Circular reasoning
C) Knowledge of a universal or indefinite proposition
D) Confusion

Key Takeaway
Two self-evident propositions, when properly arranged, yield knowledge of a universal proposition without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a belief you hold dear, do you ever assume the very thing you are trying to prove? How does this lesson on avoiding circularity expose a weakness in your own reasoning that you need to repent from?
?
If the arrangement of self-evident truths yields new knowledge, what does that say about your reliance on memorized truths without proper ordering? Will you commit to learning the logical structure of your faith from now on?

Distinguishing Premise Entailment from Reasoning Entailment
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between premise entailment and reasoning entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the entailment of premises for a conclusion and the entailment of reasoning for knowledge of that conclusion.

Core Idea
Reasoning entails knowledge, but premises entail the result; these are different.

A sophisticated objection arises: if reasoning yields knowledge, that means reasoning entails knowledge of the result. If someone denies that sound reasoning entails knowledge, how can they accept that the result follows? Al-Jurjani clarifies that the denier denies the entailment of reasoning for knowledge, but the premises themselves entail the result. This distinction is crucial in understanding logical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage from 'فإن قلت' to 'فتأمل'. Understand the objection that if reasoning entails knowledge, then denying that entails knowledge would contradict.
2
Identify the two entailments
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between the entailment of reasoning for knowledge and the entailment of premises for the result.
The denier denies the first but not the second.
3
Reflect on the difference
Notice the subtlety: the controversial entailment is about knowledge acquisition, not logical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أما الصغرى إلخ) استدلال على حقيتها بأنها بديهية لأن تصور طرفيها كاف في الحكم، وكل بديهي فهو حق، وكذا قوله وأما الكبرى إلخ، وزاد قوله لا شبهة فيها إشارة إلى أنها بديهية لا خفاء فيها أصلاً باعتبار الحكم، ولا باعتبار الطرفين بخلاف الصغرى، فإن فيها خفاء باعتبار الطرفين وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن الاعتراض بأن الاستدلال على الصغرى والكبرى ينافي دعوى بداهتهما المستفادة من قوله، وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان إلخ والجواب بأن الاستدلال المذكور تنبيه أو تعليل لمي، والبديهي قد يكون نظرياً من حيث لميته كلام منشؤه عدم التدبر فتدبر. قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) مجمل الجواب أن هاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان بأي عبارة عبرنا بهما، إذا رتبناهما ترتيباً مخصوصاً يفيد ذلك الترتيب العلم بتلك القضية الكلية أو المهملة، فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه. قوله : ( فنقول النتيجة في كل نظر قياسي إلخ) فإن قلت : معنى قولنا النظر يفيد العلم أنه يستلزم العلم بالنتيجة فمن ينكر استلزام النظر الصحيح للنتيجة كيف يسلم لزوم النتيجة؟ قلت : المنكر هو استلزام النظر للعلم بالنتيجة والمذكور هو استلزام المقدمات للنتيجة، والفرق ظاهر وبالجملة عنوان العلمية يلاحظ هاهنا في اللازم ولا عنوان النظر فقط في جانب الملزوم فلا إشكال فتأمل . قوله : ( وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان (بدیهیتان قيل دعوى بداهتهما ينافي الصغرى والكبرى، اللهم إلا أن يقال: ما ذكر تنبيه فإن قلت قوله في التحرير الأول، وأما الكبرى فبديهية لا شبهة فيها يدل على نظرية الصغرى قلت بل أراد به الكبرى بديهية لا تحتاج إلى التنبيه كما دل عليه قوله : لا شبهة فيها، ويمكن أن يقال أيضاً البديهي قد يكون نظرياً نظراً إلى لميته كما صرح به في شرح المقاصد .
His statement: (As for the minor, etc.) is a proof of its truth by its being self-evident, because conceiving its two extremes is sufficient for judgment, and every self-evident is true. And similarly his statement: 'As for the major, etc.' And he added his statement: 'no doubt in it' as an indication that it is self-evident, with no obscurity at all in terms of judgment, nor in terms of the two extremes, unlike the minor, for it has obscurity in terms of the two extremes. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the objection that proving the minor and the major contradicts the claim of their self-evidence derived from his statement 'In summary, here are two propositions, etc.' and the answer is that the mentioned proof is a reminder or a causal explanation (limmi), and the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its cause; this speech originates from lack of reflection, so reflect. His statement: (In summary, etc.) is a summary answer that here there are two self-evident propositions, in whatever expression we express them, when we arrange them in a particular order that order yields knowledge of that universal or indefinite [general] proposition, so it is not proving a thing by itself. His statement: (We say: the result in every syllogistic reasoning, etc.) For if you say: the meaning of our saying 'reasoning yields knowledge' is that it entails knowledge of the result; so how can one who denies that sound reasoning entails the result accept the entailment of the result? I say: the denier is of the entailment of reasoning for knowledge of the result, while what is mentioned is the entailment of the premises for the result, and the difference is clear. In summary, the aspect of knowledge is observed here in the necessary [consequent], and not the aspect of reasoning only in the side of the necessitating [antecedent], so no problem. Reflect. His statement: (In summary, here are two self-evident propositions.) It was said: claiming their self-evidence contradicts the minor and major [premises that are proven]. Unless it is said: what was mentioned is a reminder. For if you say: his statement in the first exposition 'As for the major, it is self-evident, no doubt in it' indicates the theoretical nature of the minor. I say: rather he meant that the major is self-evident and does not need a reminder, as indicated by his statement 'no doubt in it'. And it is also possible to say: the self-evident may be theoretical with respect to its cause, as is stated in Sharh al-Maqasid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the two self-evident propositions against objections. The commentator first explains that the proof of the minor and major premises does not contradict their self-evidence; rather, it is a reminder (tanbih) or a causal explanation (ta'lil limmi). He notes that the self-evident may be theoretical from the perspective of its cause. He then clarifies that the ordering of the two self-evident propositions yields knowledge of the universal or general proposition without circularity. Next, he addresses an objection: if someone says that the meaning of 'reasoning yields knowledge' is that it entails knowledge of the result, then how can one who denies that sound reasoning entails the result accept the entailment of the result? The answer distinguishes between the entailment of the premises for the result (which is conceded) and the entailment of reasoning for knowledge of the result (which is denied). Finally, he responds to another objection: claiming the two propositions are self-evident contradicts the minor and major premises that are proven. The reply distinguishes between the first exposition where the major is said to be self-evident (no need for reminder) and the minor which may need a reminder. The self-evident can also be theoretical with respect to its cause, as in Sharh al-Maqasid.

Key Terms
استلزام
نظر
نتيجة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that reasoning yields knowledge means denying logical consequence entirely.
Correction: The denier denies the entailment of reasoning for knowledge, but does not deny that premises entail the result.

Checkpoint
What does the denier deny according to Al-Jurjani?
A) That premises entail the result
B) That reasoning entails knowledge of the result
C) That the result follows from premises
D) That the syllogism is valid

Key Takeaway
The entailment of premises for the result is distinct from the entailment of reasoning for knowledge; denying one does not require denying the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a true conclusion because your reasoning felt shaky, even though the premises were solid? How will you distinguish your confidence in your reasoning from the logical force of the premises from now on?
?
When you present Islam to others, do you focus more on the strength of your own reasoning or on the inherent truth of the premises (like the Qur'an and Sunnah)? Which one truly carries the proof, and how does this lesson change your approach?

The Self-Evident May Need a Reminder: The Minor vs Major
Al-Jurjani distinguishes the minor and major premises in terms of need for reminder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the major premise is self-evident without need for a reminder, while the minor may require a reminder, and how this does not contradict their shared self-evidence.

Core Idea
The major premise is self-evident without need for reminder; the minor may need a reminder but is still self-evident.

The final objection reiterates the tension between claiming self-evidence and proving. Al-Jurjani responds with a nuanced point: the major premise is self-evident with no need for a reminder, as indicated by the phrase 'no doubt in it.' The minor, however, may require a reminder because it has some obscurity in its terms. Yet both are self-evident; the self-evident can be theoretical from the perspective of its cause, as referenced from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage starting with 'وبالجملة فهاهنا قضيتان بديهيتان'. Note the claim that self-evidence contradicts proving.
2
Distinguish major and minor
Al-Jurjani points out that the major is self-evident with no need for a reminder, while the minor may need a reminder due to obscurity.
This is based on the phrase 'no doubt in it' for the major.
3
Understand the 'theoretical from cause' point
The self-evident may be theoretical with respect to its cause, meaning it can be proven without losing self-evidence.
This is supported by Sharh al-Maqasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

The Self-Evident Particular Judgment: Halting Infinite Regress
Al-Jurjani explains how the particular instance of reasoning can be self-evident, preventing infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani argues that a judgment about a specific reasoning act can be self-evident, thereby preventing circularity and infinite regress in the foundation of reasoning.

Core Idea
The particular judgment is self-evident because its object is conceived in its essence, not as a general instance.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a crucial objection in the epistemology of reasoning: if every judgment about whether a reasoning act yields knowledge itself requires reasoning, we face an infinite regress or circularity. He responds by showing that the judgment about a particular reasoning act can be self-evident, requiring no further proof. This lesson unpacks his nuanced argument and the ensuing counter-objection.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the problem of regress
Understand why infinite regress threatens: if judging whether reasoning yields knowledge is itself a reasoning process, it needs another judgment, ad infinitum.
This is the background for al-Jurjani's solution.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on the distinction between conceiving a thing as a particular (mushakhkhas) and as a universal or indefinite (kulliyya/muhmala).
Notice the phrase 'difference of aspect' (ikhtilāf al-ʿunwān).
3
Identify the key claim
Al-Jurjani claims that the particular judgment is necessary (self-evident) because its conception is sufficient for judgment, unlike the universal which requires an additional aspect.
This claim stops the regress by providing a foundation.
4
Consider the counter-objection
Read the objector's point: if it were self-evident, rational people would not differ. Note al-Jurjani's response that the premise is still needed to repel the objection.
This shows the passage is a dialectical treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفيد العلم، ثم إن حكمنا بأن هذا النظر الجزئي الواقع في هاتين المقدمتين، يفيد يحتاج فيه إلا إلى تصور الطرفين من حيث خصوصهما فقط، من لا العلم بديهي غير أن يعلم أنه من أفراد النظر أو لا ، فلا يلزم حينئذ إلا توقف العلم بالقضية الكلية على العلم بالقضية الشخصية ( وقد تكون القضية ( المشخصة ضرورية ) معلومة بالضرورة كما ذكرناه من الحكم بإفادة العلم على هذا النظر الجزئي ( دون الكلية أو المهملة ) بل تكونان نظريتين وذلك جائز ) لاختلاف العنوان ) في المشخصة والكلية والمهملة فيجوز اختلافها في الضرورية والنظرية ( فإن) الحكم (البديهي مشروط بتصور الطرفين بلا شبهة ) وتصور الشيء بكونه نظراً ما كما في القضية الكلية والمهملة ( غير تصوره باعتبار ذاته المخصوصة كما في القضية المشخصة فجاز أن يكون تصوره من حيث ذاته المخصوصة مع تصور المحكوم به كافياً في الحكم بينهما، فتكون المشخصة ضروريةً ولا يكون تصوره من حيث إنه فرد من أفراد النظر كذلك، فلا تكون الكلية ولا المهملة ضرورية بل نظرية موقوفة على تلك المشخصة، ولا استحالة فيه فإن قلت : لا شك أن الكلية مشتملة على أحكام قوله : (ثم إن حكمنا إلخ) أي بعدما تحققت أن هاهنا إثباتاً للكلية أو المهملة بشخصية، لا وعلمت أنه ليس إثبات الشيء بنفسه، فاعلم أن الحكم في تلك المشخصة بديهي حتى يختلج في وهمك أن الحكم بإفادة هذا النظر الجزئي نظري لفرض الكلية أو المهملة نظرية، فيحتاج إلى نظر آخر وهو أيضا نظري فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل، فقوله : . ثم إن حكمنا إلخ دفع اعتراض يرد بعد بيان أنه ليس فيه إثبات الشيء بنفسه قوله : ( فلا يلزم حينئذ إلا توقف إلخ لا التوقف على نظر،آخر ، فلا يلزم الدور أو التسلسل . قوله: (فجاز أن يكون تصوره إلخ مثلاً إذا كان ذلك النظر الجزئي على هيئة الشكل الأول، كما مر يكون إنتاجه بيناً وإفادته للعلم بالنتيجة بديهية، فيكون تصوره كافياً في الحكم بأنه مفيد . • قوله : لا شك إلخ) يعني أن ما ذكر وإن دل على تغاير المثبت والمثبت بالكلية والجزئية، فلا يكون إثبات الشيء بنفسه لكنه يلزم ذلك بطريق آخر، وهو أنه إذا ثبت الكلية بنظر قوله : (ثم إن حكمنا إلخ) قيل لا حاجة إلى هذه المقدمة في أصل المطلوب، فإن المقدمتين لما حصلتا في الذهن مرتبتين حصل المطلوب وهو أن العلم الحاصل عقيب النظر الصحيح علم، وأما أن حال إفادة هاتين المقدمتين لذلك المطلوب ماذا ، فلا حاجة بنا إلى بيانه وإنما هو بيان للواقع، ثم للمعترض أن يعود ويقول : لو كان ضرورياً لما اختلف العقلاء فيه، ولما وجد التفاوت بينه وبين سائر البديهيات، فتضطر إلى جواب الإمام ولو أمكن منع الاختلاف في هذا الجزئي المشخص لم يمكن منع التفاوت وأنت خبير بأن المقدمة المذكورة إنما احتيج إليها دفعاً لعود الاعتراض على إفادة تينك المقدمتين للمطلوب .
[That it] yields knowledge. Then, if we judge that this particular reasoning occurring in these two premises yields knowledge, it only requires conceiving the two extremes from the aspect of their particularity only; knowledge is self-evident except that it is known whether it is an individual of reasoning or not. Then in that case, only the knowledge of the universal proposition depends on knowledge of the particular proposition. And the particular proposition may be necessary, known necessarily, as we have mentioned of the judgment that this particular reasoning yields knowledge, without the universal or indefinite [general] being necessary; rather both are theoretical, and that is permissible because of the difference of aspect. For the particular, universal, and indefinite differ, so their difference in necessity and theory is permissible. Because the self-evident judgment is conditioned by conceiving the two extremes without doubt, and conceiving a thing as being reasoning (as in the universal and indefinite) is different from conceiving it by its particular essence (as in the particular). So it is possible that its conception by its particular essence together with the conception of the predicate is sufficient for judgment between them, so the particular is necessary, and its conception by its being an individual of reasoning is not like that, so neither the universal nor the indefinite is necessary, but rather theoretical, dependent on that particular, and there is no impossibility in that. For if you say: there is no doubt that the universal includes judgments. His statement: (Then if we judge, etc.) means: after you have realized that here there is a proof of the universal or indefinite by a particular, and you know it is not proving a thing by itself, then know that the judgment in that particular is self-evident, lest it occur to your mind that the judgment that this particular reasoning yields knowledge is theoretical because the universal or indefinite is assumed theoretical, so it would need another reasoning, which is also theoretical, leading to circularity or infinite regress. So his statement: 'Then if we judge, etc.' is a refutation of an objection that occurs after explaining that it is not proving a thing by itself. His statement: (Then in that case, only the dependence, etc.) i.e., not dependence on another reasoning, so circularity or infinite regress does not occur. His statement: (So it is possible that its conception, etc.) for example, if that particular reasoning is in the form of the first figure, as has passed, its production is evident and its yielding knowledge of the result is self-evident, so its conception is sufficient for judging that it is useful. His statement: (No doubt, etc.) means that what was mentioned, although it indicates the difference between what is proved and what is proved in the universal and particular, so it is not proving a thing by itself, but that [circularity] occurs in another way: that if the universal is proved by a reasoning... His statement: (Then if we judge, etc.) It was said: there is no need for this premise in the original sought, for when the two premises are present in the mind in proper order, the sought is obtained, i.e., that the knowledge that follows sound reasoning is knowledge; as for the state of these two premises yielding that sought, we have no need to explain it; it is just an explanation of fact. Then the objector may return and say: if it were necessary, the rational people would not differ about it, and there would not be a difference between it and other self-evident things. So one is forced to [use] the answer of the Imam. Even if difference can be denied in this particular, specified [judgment], difference cannot be denied. You know well that the mentioned premise was only needed to repel the return of the objection to the yielding of those two premises for the sought.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of how a particular judgment about a specific reasoning act can be self-evident, while the universal judgment about all reasoning is theoretical. The author first states that if we judge that a particular reasoning yields knowledge, this judgment only requires conceiving the extremes in their particularity; knowledge is self-evident except for knowing whether it is an instance of reasoning or not. The universal or general judgment then depends on the particular judgment. The particular judgment can be necessary (known by necessity), while the universal and indefinite are theoretical. This is possible because the aspect (i'itibar) differs: conceiving a thing as a particular individual is different from conceiving it as a general individual of reasoning. The self-evident judgment requires conceiving the extremes without doubt; in the particular case, this condition is met, so it is self-evident. The universal, requiring an additional aspect, is theoretical and dependent on the particular. The commentary then explains that this prevents circularity and infinite regress: the particular judgment is self-evident, so no further reasoning is needed. An example from the first figure illustrates that the productivity of that particular reasoning is self-evident. Finally, a counter-objection is raised: if the particular judgment were necessary, rational people would not differ on it, and it would not differ from other self-evident things. The commentator notes that the premise was needed to fend off the return of the objection against the two original premises yielding the sought conclusion.

Key Terms
مشخصة
كلية
مهملة
ضرورية
نظرية
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All judgments about whether reasoning yields knowledge must themselves be theoretical, leading to an infinite regress.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that the judgment about a particular reasoning act can be self-evident because its object is conceived in its essence, not as a general instance, thus stopping the regress.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the particular judgment about a specific reasoning act self-evident while the universal judgment is theoretical?
A) Because the universal judgment requires additional reflection that the particular does not.
B) Because conceiving the particular by its essence is sufficient for judgment, whereas conceiving it as a general instance requires an additional aspect.
C) Because the particular judgment is based on empirical observation.
D) Because the universal judgment is false.

Key Takeaway
The particular judgment about a reasoning act can be self-evident, preventing infinite regress, because its conception is sufficient for judgment without additional reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your reasoning about a matter is universal and absolute, only to realize it was based on a particular instance? How might this distinction between particular and universal judgments change the way you evaluate your own beliefs?
?
When you face a doubt about a foundational belief, do you ever search for a proof that leads to an infinite chain of reasoning? Could this lesson help you recognize when an answer is self-evident and stop the regress?
?
If you are certain of something, can you identify whether that certainty comes from direct conception of its essence (as in a particular judgment) or from a general rule? How might this affect your confidence in that certainty?
