Objection to Simultaneity of Premises and the Conditional Analogy
The commentator presents an objection and answers it using the conditional premise analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that two premises cannot coexist in the mind, using the analogy of conditional propositions and distinguishing between attention and knowledge.

Core Idea
Two premises can coexist in the mind, just as the parts of a conditional must coexist.

This passage addresses a critical objection to the theory of reasoning (nazar). The objector claims that simultaneous attention to two premises is impossible, so knowledge cannot arise. The commentator defends the coherence of reasoning by drawing an analogy with conditional statements and by clarifying the difference between attention and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: it denies that two premises can be attended to simultaneously, so only one premise is present, which cannot produce knowledge. Note the objector's claim about 'tawajjuh' (attention).
The objector also mentions the possibility of opinion, but the core is the simultaneity issue.
2
Examine the author's response using the conditional analogy
The author responds by denying that premises cannot coexist, and gives the example of the two parts of a conditional proposition (protasis and apodosis) that must coexist for the judgment of entailment. Consider how this analogy supports the possibility of two premises being present together.
The distinction between potential and actual judgments is raised later but the primary analogy is key.
3
Understand the final refutation: attention vs. knowledge
The commentator ultimately argues that even if two attentions cannot be simultaneous, two knowledges can. This separates the faculty of attention from knowledge itself, refuting the objection.
The phrase 'التوجه غير العلم' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقصود امتنع منا في تلك الحالة التوجه إلى حكم آخر بالوجدان، وحينئذ لم يتحقق نظر مفيد للعلم إذ المقدمة الواحدة لا تنتج اتفاقا وهذه منقوضة بإفادة النظر للظن إذا كانت متفقا عليها بخلاف الشبهة الأولى والسابقة، فإن الظن الضروري قد يظهر خطؤه، ويجوز اختلاف العقلاء فيه وتفاوته بالنسبة إلى ظن آخر. قلنا: لا نسلم أنه لا يجتمع مقدمتان في الذهن بل قد يجتمعان وذلك كطرفي الشرطية فإنهما قضيتان يجب اجتماعهما في الذهن، ولولا اجتماعهما فيه لامتنع الحكم بينهما بالتلازم أي اللزوم في المتصلات والعناد في المنفصلات. ومنهم من فرق بأن طرفي الشرطية قضيتان بالقوة لا حكم بالفعل في شيء منهما بخلاف مقدمتي النظر ونحن نعلم بالضرورة أن الحكم في إحداهما لا يجامع الحكم في الأخرى دفعة، ثم أجاب فيهما النظر في الذهن والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله، أما الملازمة فلأن الموصل مجموع المقدمتين دون إحداهما، وأما بطلان التالي فلان توجه النفس قصداً إلى حكمين في زمان واحد محال، وحاصل الجواب منع بطلان التالي مستنداً بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يجتمعا في الذهن؟ كاجتماع طرفي الشرطية، ولما كان منع المقدمة المدللة غير صحيح، أشار إلى أن منعها باعتبار أن دليلها غير مثبت لها فهي في الحقيقة غير مدللة، وذلك لأن التوجه غير العلم، ولا يلزم من امتناع اجتماع التوجهين امتناع اجتماع العلمين.
The objector said: In that case, it is impossible for us to direct attention to another judgment by inner experience. Then no reasoning beneficial for knowledge occurs, since a single premise does not produce a conclusion by agreement. This is refuted by the fact that reasoning can produce opinion if the premise is agreed upon, unlike the first and previous objection, for necessary opinion may appear erroneous, and it is possible for rational people to differ in it and for it to vary relative to another opinion. We said: We do not concede that two premises cannot coexist in the mind; rather, they can coexist, like the two parts of a conditional proposition, for they are two judgments that must coexist in the mind. Were they not to coexist therein, the judgment between them of entailment (i.e., implication in conditionals) and opposition (in disjunctives) would be impossible. And some made a distinction: the two parts of a conditional are potential judgments, having no actual judgment in either, unlike the two premises of reasoning, and we know by necessity that the judgment in one does not coexist with the judgment in the other simultaneously. Then he answered: Both premises are present in the mind, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the implication, because the connector is the sum of both premises, not one of them. As for the falsity of the consequent, because the soul's intentional attention to two judgments in one moment is impossible. The gist of the answer is the denial of the falsity of the consequent, based on: Why should it not be possible for them to coexist in the mind? Like the coexistence of the two parts of a conditional. And since the denial of the premise that is supported by evidence is not correct, he indicated that its denial is because its evidence does not prove it, so it is in reality not supported by evidence. That is because attention is not knowledge, and the impossibility of the coexistence of two attentions does not entail the impossibility of the coexistence of two knowledges.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the view that a correct reasoning (nazar) that leads to knowledge requires the simultaneous presence of both premises in the mind. The objector argues that in a given case, one cannot direct attention to another judgment (i.e., cannot attend to two judgments at once), so only one premise is present, which cannot produce knowledge. The objector further claims that if reasoning could produce opinion, it would be refuted by the fact that necessary opinion can be mistaken. The answer begins with 'We said: We do not concede that two premises cannot coexist in the mind; rather, they can coexist, like the two parts of a conditional proposition.' This is the core response: the two parts of a conditional (protasis and apodosis) must be present together for the relation of implication or disjunction to be judged. Some scholars offered a distinction: the parts of a conditional are only potential judgments (not actual), whereas the premises of reasoning are actual judgments, and it is impossible for two actual judgments to coexist simultaneously. The commentator then answers this distinction by arguing that the two premises of reasoning are present in the mind (even if not as actual judgments?), and the objection's consequent (that attention to two judgments in one moment is impossible) is false. The answer further explains that the impossibility of the coexistence of two attentions (tawajjuhayn) does not imply the impossibility of the coexistence of two knowledges ('ilmayn), because attention is not the same as knowledge. Thus, the original objection is refuted.

نظر (nazar)
مقدمة (muqaddima)
شرطية (shartiyya)
توجه (tawajjuh)
علم ('ilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that premises must be simultaneously attended to in the mind for reasoning to be valid.
Correction: The commentator shows that premises can coexist even if attention cannot be directed to both at once, and that knowledge does not require simultaneous attention.

Checkpoint

B) The two parts of a conditional proposition
C) The two premises of a categorical syllogism
D) The two sides of a contradiction

Key Takeaway
Two premises can coexist in the mind, as shown by the conditional analogy, and attention is distinct from knowledge, so the impossibility of simultaneous attention does not contradict the coexistence of knowledge.

Sequential Presence of Premises Suffices for Reasoning
Commentator answers that sequential presence of premises is sufficient for reasoning to produce knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sequential presence of premises is sufficient for reasoning to produce knowledge, and the distinction between knowledge and attention in the reasoning process.

Core Idea
Sequential presence, not simultaneous, is enough for reasoning to yield knowledge.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key objection against the possibility of knowledge through reasoning. The objection claims that two premises cannot be present together in the mind, making reasoning impossible. The answer clarifies that simultaneous presence is not required; a sequential chain of attention suffices. This lesson unpacks that answer and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'attention' and 'knowledge'.
2
Identify the main claim
The commentator states that simultaneous presence is not required. What is the sufficient condition?
Look for 'يكفيه حصول إحداهما عقيب الأخرى بلا فصل'.
3
Distinguish knowledge from attention
Understand that attention (tawajjuh) to a premise is not the same as knowledge ('ilm) of it. Reasoning is an act of attention, not knowledge itself.
The text says 'التوجه إلى مقدمة غير العلم بها'.
4
Consider the analogy
The commentator uses the example of the two parts of a conditional statement. Explain how this analogy supports the argument.
Think about how we understand a conditional without attending to both parts at once.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the main idea.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب عن الشبهة بأنه لا يجب في الإنتاج اجتماع المقدمتين معاً، بل يكفيه حصول إحداهما عقيب الأخرى بلا فصل، إذ بذلك يتحقق النظر فيهما أعني الحركة المعدة لحصول النتيجة والتوجه إلى مقدمة غير العلم بها بل هو أي التوجه إليها هو النظر فيها وملاحظتها قصداً ولا يلزم من عدم اجتماع النظرين أي التوجهين إلى المقدمتين وملاحظتيهما القصديتين عدم اجتماع العلمين بالمقدمتين، والحاصل، إن التفات النفس إلى المقدمتين معاً دفعة بالقصد ممتنع، وأما حضورهما عند النفس بأن تلاحظ إحداهما قصداً، وتتوجه بالقصد إلى الأخرى عقيب الأولى بلا فصل، فيحضران معاً وإن لم تكونا ملحوظتين قصداً دفعة كطرفي الشرطية، فليس ممتنعاً وحضورهما على هذا الوجه هو المحتاج إليه في الإنتاج، وتوضيح هذا بتشبيه المعقول بالمحسوس.
Then he answered the objection: it is not required for production that the two premises be together simultaneously; rather, it suffices that one of them occurs immediately after the other without interval, for thereby reasoning about them is realized, i.e., the movement prepared for obtaining the result, and attention to a premise is not knowledge of it; rather, it (attention) is the reasoning about it and intentionally noticing it. And the non-coexistence of the two nazars (i.e., the two attentions) to the two premises and their intentional noticing does not entail the non-coexistence of the two knowledges of the two premises. The conclusion: the soul's turning to both premises together at once intentionally is impossible. As for their presence in the soul such that one notices one intentionally and then intentionally attends to the other immediately after the first without interval, they are present together even if they are not both intentionally noticed at once, like the two parts of a conditional. This is not impossible, and their presence in this manner is what is needed for production. The clarification of this is by analogy with the sensible.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage provides a second answer to the same objection. The objection claimed that two premises cannot be together in the mind, so reasoning that leads to knowledge is impossible. The commentator now clarifies that simultaneous presence is not necessary; rather, what is required for production (intaj) is that one premise occurs immediately after the other with no gap. This sequence ('aqiba al-ukhra bi-la fasl) constitutes a single reasoning act (nazar) that moves from one premise to the next to produce the conclusion. The commentator distinguishes between knowledge ('ilm) of a premise and attention (tawajjuh) to it. Attention is the act of reasoning (nazar) and intentional noticing. The impossibility of two simultaneous attentions does not imply the impossibility of two simultaneous knowledges; the mind can hold both premises as known even if it attends to them sequentially. The commentator draws an analogy with the parts of a conditional proposition, which are also not both attended to at once but are known together. Thus, the soul's presence of both premises sequentially is sufficient for reasoning to yield knowledge. The passage ends with a note that this can be clarified by analogy with sensible objects.

توجه (tawajjuh)

نظر (nazar)
ملاحظة (mulāhaza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both premises must be present simultaneously in the mind for reasoning to produce knowledge.
Correction: The commentator explains that sequential presence of premises, one immediately after the other, is sufficient for reasoning to yield knowledge. Simultaneous mental coexistence is not required.

Checkpoint

B) One premise must be present immediately after the other without interval

Key Takeaway
Sequential presence of premises, not simultaneous attention, is sufficient for reasoning to produce knowledge, as long as they occur without interval.

The Third Objection: Knowledge of Absence of Opposing Evidence
Commentator presents and answers the third objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against inquiry yielding knowledge, and the commentator's response that it denies knowledge of validity, not the knowledge itself.

Core Idea
The third objection attacks knowing that knowledge is knowledge, not the knowledge itself.

Having established that inquiry can indeed yield knowledge, the commentator now addresses a subtle objection: even if inquiry produces knowledge, how can the inquirer be sure that his result is knowledge without knowing that no counterargument exists? This objection, if unanswered, would undermine the certainty of rational inquiry. The commentator distinguishes between the knowledge itself and the knower's awareness of its validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection raises a logical problem: if inquiry yields knowledge, the inquirer must also know that there is no opposing evidence, otherwise he cannot be certain that his result is knowledge.
The passage includes both the objection and the commentator's interlinear glosses.
2
Identify the core logical issue
The objection argues that without knowing the absence of opposing evidence, the inquirer faces a dilemma: certainty in both would require believing contradictories, and certainty in one without the other would be arbitrary. This threatens the claim that inquiry yields knowledge.
Remember the principle: preponderance without a preponderator is irrational.
3
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator clarifies that the objection actually denies knowledge of the knowledge's validity (al-ʿilm bil-ifāda), not the knowledge itself. He explains that even if the inquirer allows the possibility of opposing evidence, the knowledge can still be genuine, as long as no actual opposing evidence exists.
The commentator's key move: subjective doubt does not affect objective knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction protects the certainty of knowledge from the inquirer's potential ignorance. The knowledge remains valid if based on a sound proof and no actual counterevidence, regardless of the inquirer's awareness.
This is a sophisticated epistemological point that preserves the objectivity of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة النظر لو أفاد العلم وعلم أن ذلك المفاد علم فمع العلم بعدم المعارض المقاوم إذ معه أي مع المعارض وظهوره للناظر يحصل التوقف لأن الجزم بمقتضاهما يوجب اعتقاد النقيضين وبمقتضى أحدهما دون الآخر يوجب الترجيح بلا مرجح، فإذا لم يعلم عدم المعارض وجوز وجوده لم يعلم أن. قوله: (وعلم أن ذلك إلخ) إشارة إلى أن الشبهة المذكورة تفيد نفي العلم بالإفادة، لا نفي نفس الإفادة كما سيظهر لك. قوله: (فمع العلم أي يفيده مع العلم بعدم المعارض لا بنفسه فقط. قوله: أي) مع المعارض وظهوره يعني أن الضمير راجع إلى المعارض، والكلام على حذف المضاف أعني الظهور بقرينة أن حصول التوقف للناظر، إنما يترتب على ظهور المعارض له لا على وجوده في نفس الأمر. قوله: (فإذا لم يعلم إلخ) أي إذا كان ظهور المعارض موجباً للتوقف، فإذا لم يعلم عدم المعارض وجوز وجوده لم يعلم أن المفاد علم، وإن كان علماً في نفسه، وذلك لأن جواز وجود المعارض عند الناظر لا ينافي الجزم بالحكم المفاد بالنظر، إنما ينافيه وجود المعارض بالفعل، فيجوز أن يحصل له الجزم بالحكم بالنظر ويكون مطابقاً للواقع لعدم المعارض فيه وثابتاً لاستناده إلى الدليل مع تجويزه للمعارض لقدم العلم بعدمه إما بالفعل بأن يكون متردداً أو بالقوة بأن يكون خالي الذهن، فلا يحصل العلم بأنه علم لعدم الجزم بثباته، وبهذا ظهر أن الشبهة.
The third objection: Inquiry (al-nazar), if it yields knowledge and one knows that that result is knowledge, then [it requires] knowledge of the absence of opposing evidence (al-muʿāriḍ al-muqāwim), for with it (i.e., with the opposing evidence and its appearing to the inquirer) hesitation occurs, because being certain about both of them necessitates believing in two contradictories, and being certain about one without the other necessitates preponderance without a preponderator. So if he does not know the absence of opposing evidence and allows its possibility, he does not know that... His statement: 'and he knows that that etc.' indicates that the mentioned objection negates knowledge of the validity (al-ʿilm bil-ifāda), not the very validity itself, as will appear to you. His statement: 'so with knowledge' i.e., it yields [knowledge] with knowledge of the absence of opposing evidence, not by itself alone. His statement: 'i.e.' with the opposing evidence and its appearing, meaning that the pronoun refers to the opposing evidence, and the phrase has an omitted genitive, namely 'the appearing', by evidence that the occurrence of hesitation for the inquirer results from the appearing of the opposing evidence to him, not from its existence in actual reality. His statement: 'so if he does not know etc.' i.e., if the appearing of opposing evidence necessitates hesitation, then if he does not know the absence of opposing evidence and allows its possibility, he does not know that the result is knowledge, even if it is knowledge in itself, because the possibility of opposing evidence in the inquirer's mind does not contradict certainty in the judgment yielded by inquiry; only actual existence of opposing evidence contradicts it. So it is possible for him to obtain certainty in the judgment by inquiry, and it conforms to reality due to the absence of opposing evidence therein, and it is established due to its reliance on proof, despite his allowing the possibility of opposing evidence due to his prior lack of knowledge of its absence—either actually, by being hesitant, or potentially, by being mentally blank. Thus he does not obtain knowledge that it is knowledge because he lacks certainty of its stability. And by this it appears that the objection...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the view that inquiry yields knowledge. The objection states: if inquiry yields knowledge, and one knows that the result is knowledge, then it requires knowing the absence of any opposing evidence that would cause hesitation. Because if opposing evidence appears, the reasoner would become uncertain. If the reasoner does not know whether there is opposing evidence, then he cannot know that his result is knowledge, even if it is true knowledge in itself. The commentator responds by clarifying that the objection actually attacks the knowledge of the validity of the knowledge, not the knowledge itself. The result can still be genuine knowledge in reality, even if the inquirer is not certain of its status due to the possibility of unknown opposing evidence. The certainty of the knowledge is not affected by the inquirer's subjective doubt; rather, the knowledge remains valid as long as it is based on a valid proof and there is no actual opposing evidence, regardless of the inquirer's awareness.

Key Terms
الشبهة الثالثة
المعارض
العلم بالإفادة
التوقف
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection shows that inquiry cannot yield knowledge unless the inquirer is absolutely certain that no opposing evidence exists.
Correction: The commentator demonstrates that the objection only affects knowing that one knows, not the knowledge itself. The inquirer's lack of awareness does not undermine the reality of the knowledge if it is based on sound proof and no actual counterevidence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the third objection ultimately deny?
A) That inquiry can produce knowledge at all.
B) That one can know that one's result is knowledge, without denying the knowledge itself.
C) That knowledge must be based on evidence.
D) That opposing evidence is possible.

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails to disprove that inquiry yields knowledge; it only shows that the inquirer may not be certain that he knows, yet the knowledge itself remains valid if based on a sound proof without actual counterevidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain about a truth—like a belief in Allah or a principle of faith—but then doubted because you thought there might be a counterargument you haven't considered? How does this lesson's distinction between knowledge itself and knowing that you know address that fear? What would it take for you to act on that knowledge despite the lingering possibility of objections?
?
When you recite 'I know that there is no god but Allah,' do you also require yourself to know that you know it? Is there a difference in your heart between the certainty of that belief and the quiet worry that you might be deceiving yourself? Let this lesson push you to examine: Are you living as if you truly know, or merely hoping that you know?

The Third Doubt About Reasoning and Its Answer
Author presents third doubt about nazar and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of infinite regress against reasoning is refuted by the claim that correct definitive reasoning inherently provides knowledge of the absence of opposing evidence.

Core Idea
Correct definitive reasoning yields necessary knowledge of no opponent.

The third doubt attacks the reliability of reasoning by arguing that certainty requires knowing there is no counterargument, which would lead to an infinite regress. The answer shows that correct reasoning in definitive premises automatically provides that knowledge, without needing a separate proof. This lesson walks through the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow of the objection and then the response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection's logical chain
The objection states that if reasoning provides knowledge, one must know there is no opponent. But that knowledge would also need reasoning, leading to regress. Find each link: knowledge requires no opponent → absence is not necessary → regress.
Note the key terms: mu'arid, nazari, tasalsul.
3
See how the answer resolves the regress
The answer says that correct reasoning in definitive premises provides knowledge of no opponent necessarily, just as it provides knowledge of the result. No extra reasoning is needed.
Contrast the answer's claim with the objection's assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة النظر لو أفاد العلم وعلم أن ذلك المفاد علم ( فمع العلم بعدم المعارض ( المقاوم (إذ معه ) أي مع المعارض وظهوره للناظر ( يحصل التوقف ) لأن الجزم بمقتضاهما يوجب اعتقاد النقيضين وبمقتضى أحدهما دون الآخر يوجب الترجيح بلا مرجح، فإذا لم يعلم عدم المعارض وجوز وجوده لم يعلم أن ما أفاده النظر علم، وحق، بل جوز كون نقيضه حقاً ( وعدمه ليس ضرورياً وإلا لم يقع المعارض أي لم ينكشف وجوده بعد النظر، وكثيراً ما ينكشف ( فهو نظري ويحتاج إلى نظر آخر) يفيده (وهو) أي ذلك النظر الآخر (أيضاً محتمل لقيام المعارض، فلا يعلم أيضاً أن ما أفاده علم وحق إلا بعد العلم بعدم ما يعارضه، وليس ضرورياً بل نظري يحتاج إلى نظر ثالث ( ويتسلسل) فيتوقف حصول العلم من النظر على أنظار غير متناهية قلنا : النظر الصحيح في المقدمات القطعية كما يفيد العلم بحقية النتيجة يفيد العلم بعد المعارض يعني كما أن العلم بأن النتيجة حقة أي بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل بعدم النظر علم متوقف على وجود النظر حاصل بعده بطريق الضرورة دون الكسب، وظهور الخطأ فيه بعد النظر الصحيح القطعي ممنوع على ما مر كذلك العلم بعدم المعارض ضروري حاصل بعد ذلك النظر، وانكشاف
The third doubt: if reasoning provides knowledge and it is known that what is provided is knowledge, then with knowledge of the absence of an opponent (the resistant one, since with it, i.e., with the opponent and its appearance to the reasoner, hesitation occurs), because affirming both entails belief in two contradictories, and affirming one without the other entails preponderance without a preponderator. So if he does not know the absence of an opponent and permits its existence, he does not know that what reasoning provided is knowledge and truth; rather, he permits that its opposite is true. And its absence is not necessary, otherwise the opponent would not occur, i.e., its existence would not be uncovered after reasoning, and often it is uncovered. So it is theoretical and needs another reasoning that provides it, and that other reasoning is also susceptible to the existence of an opponent, so he also does not know that what it provided is knowledge and truth except after knowledge of the absence of what opposes it, and it is not necessary but theoretical needing a third reasoning, and it regresses, so the attainment of knowledge from reasoning depends on infinite reasonings. We say: correct reasoning in definitive premises, just as it provides knowledge of the truth of the result, provides knowledge of the absence of an opponent. That is, just as the knowledge that the result is true, i.e., that the belief obtained after reasoning is knowledge, depends on the existence of reasoning and is obtained after it by way of necessity, not acquisition, and the emergence of error in it after correct definitive reasoning is prevented as previously mentioned, so too knowledge of the absence of an opponent is necessary and obtained after that reasoning, and uncovering...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (shubha) against the claim that nazar (reasoning) yields knowledge, and then provides the answer (jawab). The objection is the third doubt. It argues that if nazar provides knowledge, and one knows that the result is knowledge, then one must also know that there is no opponent evidence (mu'arid) that would contradict it. Otherwise, if the reasoner does not know of the absence of an opponent and merely allows for its existence, he cannot be certain that the result is knowledge and true; he might even think its opposite is true. The objection further claims that knowledge of the absence of an opponent is not necessary (otherwise opponents would never arise), but is theoretical (nazari) and requires another nazar. That second nazar also faces the same problem, leading to an infinite regress. The answer (beginning with 'qulna') states that correct nazar applied to definitive premises (muqaddimat qat'iyya) not only provides knowledge of the truth of the result, but also provides knowledge of the absence of an opponent. Just as knowledge of the result's truth is obtained necessarily after that nazar, not by acquisition (kasb), so too knowledge of the absence of an opponent is necessary and obtained after that nazar. The passage thus refutes the regress objection by affirming that knowledge of no opponent is a necessary byproduct of correct definitive reasoning.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
معارض (mu'arid)
علم (knowledge)
شبهة (shubha)
ضروري (necessary)
نظري (theoretical)
تسلسل (regress)
مقدمات قطعية (definitive premises)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that to be certain of a conclusion, one must separately prove that there is no counterargument, leading to an infinite chain of proofs.
Correction: The answer clarifies that correct definitive reasoning inherently yields the knowledge that no counterargument exists—it does not require an additional proof.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what does correct nazar in definitive premises provide besides knowledge of the truth of the result?
A) Knowledge that the opponent actually exists.
B) Knowledge of the absence of an opponent (mu'arid).
C) The ability to guess the opponent's argument.
D) A probabilistic opinion about the result.

Key Takeaway
Correct definitive reasoning necessarily provides knowledge of the absence of any counterargument, eliminating the need for an infinite regress of proofs.

Are Knowledge of Conclusion's Truth and Absence of Counterargument Necessary?
Discussion on whether knowledge of the conclusion's truth and absence of counterargument is necessary or theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that the knowledge of the absence of a counterargument can be considered necessary (ḍarūrī) despite depending on premises, as long as it is derived through an innate syllogism (fiṭrī al-qiyās) rather than deliberate acquisition (kasbī).

Core Idea
Necessity of knowledge depends on how it is derived, not just its content.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif tackles a subtle point in the epistemology of reasoning: whether the knowledge that a conclusion is correct must itself be necessary or can be theoretical. The commentator clarifies that even if the knowledge of the absence of a counterargument is derived from premises, it can still be necessary if the derivation is innate rather than acquired. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Read the passage to find the main issue: Is the knowledge that the conclusion is true and that there is no counterargument necessary or theoretical?
2
Understand the commentator's position
The commentator argues that the knowledge of the absence of a counterargument can be necessary if it comes via innate syllogism. Note the distinction between iktisāb (acquisition) and fiṭrī al-qiyās (innate syllogism).
This is the key point.
3
Examine the objection and response
The passage includes an objection that knowledge depending on premises cannot be intuitive. The commentator responds that it depends on how the premises are used. If the derivation is automatic (innate), it remains necessary.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the structure of knowledge in kalam. It shows that necessity is not solely about content but about the mode of cognition.

His statement: (obtained after it by way of necessity) means that it is a necessary entailment (lāzim bayyin) in the more general sense, as he depicted it at the end of the discourse. Commentary
This passage is a discussion about whether the knowledge that the conclusion is true and the knowledge that there is no counterargument are necessary (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī). The commentator explains the author's statement. He argues that if the knowledge that the conclusion is true depends on the knowledge of the absence of a counterargument, and that latter knowledge is acquired (kasbī), then the former cannot be necessary. However, he clarifies that the term 'necessary' here does not mean 'certain' but refers to an immediate entailment. The commentator further addresses a potential objection: the absence of knowledge of a counterargument does not imply the existence of a counterargument; what is required is the non-existence of the counterargument, not knowledge of it. He also discusses a criticism about the intuitive nature of this knowledge, concluding that even if it depends on premises, it can still be necessary if the derivation is not through deliberate acquisition but innate syllogism. The passage ends by referencing the commentator's earlier discussion on the objection to self-evident principles.

Correction: Knowledge derived from premises can still be necessary if the derivation is through an innate syllogism (fiṭrī al-qiyās) rather than deliberate acquisition (kasbī). The necessity is about the mode of cognition, not the absence of premises.

The Fourth Objection: Entailment of Knowledge by Reasoning
The fourth objection and its response regarding the entailment of knowledge by reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against reasoning yielding knowledge and how the Ashʿarī response distinguishes between simultaneous entailment and habitual subsequent occurrence.

Core Idea
Reasoning entails knowledge not as a cause, but as a habitually subsequent occurrence.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if reasoning produces knowledge, it must do so either necessarily or not; both seem problematic. The response introduces a subtle distinction that saves the Ashʿarī position. Let's unpack this important argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: (1) if reasoning entails knowledge, it violates the condition that knowledge is absent during reasoning; (2) if it does not entail knowledge, it is useless.
Notice how the first horn uses the logical principle that if A entails B, then absence of B implies absence of A.
2
Identify the response's key distinction
The response distinguishes between simultaneous causation (like hand and key) and subsequent occurrence (istiʿqāb). The entailment meant here is the latter: knowledge follows reasoning after its completion, not during it.
3
Connect to the condition
Because knowledge occurs after reasoning ends, the condition that knowledge is absent during reasoning remains valid. There is no contradiction.
This preserves the Ashʿarī view that correct reasoning leads to knowledge habitually.
4
Reflect on the examples
The examples from Ashʿarī (habit), philosophers (preparation), and Muʿtazila (generation) all illustrate that knowledge follows reasoning without simultaneous coexistence. Consider how each school explains the connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة إما أن يستلزم العلم بالمنظور فيه أولا والأول ينافي كون عدم العلم بالمنظور فيه شرطاً له لأن عدم اللازم مناف لوجود الملزوم، فلا يكون شرطاً له لكن عدم العلم بالمنظور فيه شرط للنظر، لئلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل على ما سيأتي والثاني وهو أن لا يستلزم النظر العلم بالمنظور فيه هو المطلوب قلنا : يستلزمه أنه يستعقبه عادة كما هو مذهبنا أو إعداداً أو توليداً على مذهب الحكماء والمعتزلة، فإذا تم النظر حصل العلم كما أنه إذا تمت الحركة الحسية وصل إلى المكان الذي قصد بها الحصول فيه لا بمعنى أنه يعني النظر علة موجبة له كإيجاب حركة اليد حركة المفتاح حتى يلزم اجتماعهما في الزمان معا وذلك الاستلزام الذي هو بمعنى الاستعقاب لا ينافي كون عدم العلم بالمنظور فيه شرط له.
The fourth doubt: Either (reasoning) entails knowledge of the object of reasoning, or not. The first contradicts that absence of knowledge of the object of reasoning is a condition for it, because the absence of the necessary is incompatible with the existence of the necessitated, so it would not be a condition for it; but absence of knowledge of the object of reasoning is a condition for reasoning, lest attainment of the attained occur, as will come. The second, that reasoning does not entail knowledge of the object of reasoning, is the required. We said: It entails it in the sense that it is followed by it (yastaʿqibuhu) habitually, as is our doctrine, or as preparation or generation according to the philosophers and Muʿtazila. So when reasoning is completed, knowledge occurs, just as when a sensory movement is completed, one reaches the place intended to be attained by it—not meaning that reasoning is an efficient cause of it, like the movement of the hand causing the movement of the key such that they would be simultaneous in time. And that entailment which is in the sense of subsequent occurrence (istiʿqāb) does not contradict that absence of knowledge of the object of reasoning is a condition for it.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection (shubha rābiʿa) against the claim that correct reasoning (al-naẓar) yields knowledge, along with the response. The objection argues: If reasoning entails knowledge of its object, then the condition that knowledge is absent during reasoning is violated, because necessary accompaniment (istilzām) would require both to exist simultaneously. If it does not entail knowledge, then reasoning is useless. The respondent (presumably al-Ījī or al-Jurjānī) answers by distinguishing between two meanings of 'entailment': (1) simultaneous causal necessity (like hand and key), and (2) habitual or preparatory subsequent occurrence (istiʿqāb). The latter is meant here: reasoning prepares the mind such that knowledge follows after its completion, not during it. Thus, the condition of absence of knowledge holds during the process, and the knowledge arrives afterward, avoiding contradiction. The examples from Ashʿarī (ʿādah), philosophers (iʿdād), and Muʿtazila (tawlīd) illustrate that knowledge follows reasoning without simultaneous coexistence.

Key Terms
النظر (al-naẓar)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الاستلزام (al-istilzām)
الاستعقاب (al-istiʿqāb)
العادة (al-ʿādah)
التوليد (al-tawlīd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if reasoning entails knowledge, it must be as an efficient, simultaneous cause, like a hand turning a key.
Correction: The response clarifies that the entailment is one of habitual subsequent occurrence (istiʿqāb), not simultaneous causation, so knowledge arises after reasoning is complete, preserving the condition that knowledge is absent during reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in response to the fourth objection?
A) Between 'entailment' as simultaneous causation and 'entailment' as subsequent occurrence.
B) Between reasoning about sensory objects and reasoning about intelligibles.
C) Between knowledge as conceptual and knowledge as assent.
D) Between necessary and contingent knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection is resolved by understanding that reasoning is followed by knowledge habitually, not that it is produced concurrently.

The Fifth Objection: Seeking What Is Known
Fifth objection and its response using taṣawwur/taṣdīq distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth objection against reasoning is resolved by distinguishing conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) from assent knowledge (taṣdīq).

Core Idea
The sought is known conceptually but not assented to, so reasoning is not impossible.

The fifth objection presents a dilemma: if the conclusion is already known, seeking it is redundant; if unknown, it cannot be recognized. This threatens the very possibility of reasoning. The answer, as you will see, relies on a crucial distinction between two types of knowledge that every student of kalām must master.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the objection and response become clear.
Notice the compact style typical of kalām texts.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection sets up a dichotomy: if the sought is known, seeking it is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); if unknown, it cannot be recognized. Understand why each horn is problematic.
3
Focus on the response's key distinction
The response introduces the distinction between conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) and assent knowledge (taṣdīq). Notice how this breaks the dilemma: the sought is conceptually known, so it can be recognized, but not yet assented to, so seeking it is meaningful.
The answer is limited to assensual matters because the debate concerns reasoning in assent propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة المطلوب إما معلوم فلا يطلب لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل، أو غير معلوم فلا يعرف أنه المطلوب، قلنا هو معلوم تصوراً غير معلوم تصديقاً، فيتميز المطلوب عند حصوله عن غيره بتصور طرفيه فيعرف أنه المطلوب، وإنما خص الجواب بالمطلوب التصديقي، لأن المتنازع فيه هو النظر الواقع في التصديقات كما أشرنا إليه.
The fifth doubt: The sought is either known, so it is not sought, because attainment of the attained is impossible; or it is unknown, so it would not be recognized as the sought. We said: It is known conceptually (taṣawwuran) but not known as assent (taṣdīqan). So the sought, when it occurs, is distinguished from others by the conception of its two terms, and thus it is recognized as the sought. The answer is specified to the assensual sought because the disputed matter is reasoning occurring in assents, as we have indicated.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection (shubha khāmisah) and its answer. The objection is a classic dilemma: if the sought (al-maṭlūb) is already known, seeking it is absurd (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); if it is unknown, one cannot recognize it when found. The response draws a crucial distinction between two types of knowledge: conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) and assent knowledge (taṣdīq). The sought is known conceptually (its terms are understood) but not assented to (the truth of the proposition is not yet affirmed). Thus, when reasoning produces assent, one recognizes it as the sought because its conceptual components are already familiar. The answer is limited to assensual (taṣdīqī) matters because the debate about reasoning (al-naẓar) in kalam concerns assents, not pure concepts.

Key Terms
المطلوب (al-maṭlūb)
التصور (al-taṣawwur)
التصديق (al-taṣdīq)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection proves reasoning impossible.
Correction: The response shows that only complete ignorance or complete knowledge would cause problems; partial conceptual knowledge is sufficient to recognize the result when attained.

Checkpoint
How does the response resolve the fifth objection?
A) By denying that reasoning produces knowledge.
B) By claiming the sought is known by analogy.
C) By distinguishing between conceptual knowledge and assent knowledge.
D) By asserting that all sought after things are already known intuitively.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection is overcome by distinguishing conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) from assent knowledge (taṣdīq), allowing the sought to be recognized even though its truth is not yet known.

The Sixth Objection: Indication and Circularity
The sixth objection about circular dependence and the response distinguishing indication types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth objection against reasoning's dependence on knowledge of indication and the response that distinguishes between technical indication and loose usage (tasāmuḥ).

Core Idea
The objection's circularity dissolves once we distinguish the 'aspect' from the relation.

This lesson presents the sixth doubt raised against the method of reasoning: if reasoning yields knowledge only if we already know how the evidence indicates, then either we already have that knowledge (making reasoning redundant) or we don't (making recognition impossible). The response clarifies that 'indication' is used loosely to mean 'leading to', and the required prior knowledge is merely conceptual knowledge of the terms, not the relational knowledge that would cause circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the two branches of the objection. Notice the logical structure: if dependence on prior knowledge, then either known from the same aspect or not.
The objection is presented in the first part of the passage.
2
Understand the two branches
The first branch leads to impossibility of seeking (attainment of the attained). The second leads to inability to recognize knowledge after attainment. Identify the implications.
The second branch references 'previously in conception' as an earlier point.
3
Examine the response
The commentator explains that 'indication' (dalālah) is used loosely (tasāmuḥ) to mean 'leading to' (īṣāl). This avoids the technical circularity because the intended meaning is that the evidence leads to the result, not that the indication as a quality is known.
The substitution of cause for effect is a rhetorical device for brevity.
4
Focus on the aspect (ḥaythiyyah)
The crucial point is that knowledge of the indicated insofar as it is indicated is assent knowledge. Knowledge of the relation (indication) would require prior knowledge of the indicated as related, which is circular. But the required prior knowledge is only conceptual knowledge of the terms, not the relation itself.
This repels the objection that circularity is inescapable.
5
Note the incomplete ending
The Arabic text breaks off at 'وما'. The argument continues in the original work. The commentary helps infer the completion: the prior knowledge needed is conceptual (taṣawwur), not the relational knowledge that would cause a circle.
Be aware that this passage is a fragment; the full response continues.

The sixth doubt: That the indication of the evidence, i.e., the reasoning in it yielding knowledge of the indicated, if it depends on knowledge of its indication to it, then it is either known from the aspect sought by reasoning or not known from that aspect. The first entails impossibility of seeking it by reasoning, let alone yielding it, because attainment of the attained is impossible. The second entails that after occurrence one would not know that it is knowledge of the sought. Thus it is apparent that it cannot be said, in refuting the second branch, that it is not sought because of impossibility of directing towards it, as previously in conception. His saying: 'i.e., the reasoning in it yielding knowledge, etc.': It is not hidden that indication (dalālah) is a quality of the evidence, and the yielding of reasoning is a quality of reasoning; it is valid to define one by the other. The commentator in such expressions treats the speech as tolerant, so the intended meaning is that the evidence leads to it (mūṣil ilayhi), as he stated later. He commits tolerance by substituting the cause for the effect absolutely, for prolixity in presenting the doubt. For if he had taken 'indication' as 'leading to', the presentation would be: If reasoning in the evidence yields knowledge, then the evidence indicates it, i.e., leads to it, because the yielding of knowledge by reasoning in the evidence entails its being leading to it, contrary to if it were said: if reasoning yields knowledge, then its yielding is either... etc. Then know that the qualification of aspect (ḥaythiyyah) is intended, i.e., knowledge of the indicated insofar as it is indicated, which is assent knowledge (ʿilm taṣdīqī). So the upshot: That reasoning yields knowledge of the indicated insofar as it is indicated, if it depends on knowledge of its indication, circularity would arise because knowledge of the evidence's indication to the indicated insofar as it is indicated depends on knowledge of the indicated insofar as it is indicated, because knowledge of a relation depends on knowledge of the two relata insofar as they are two relata. Thus what was said is repelled: that what knowledge of the relation depends on is conceptual knowledge of the indicated, etc.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection (shubha sādisa) and a detailed response. The objection argues that if reasoning's yielding knowledge depends on prior knowledge of the evidence's indication, then either that prior knowledge is from the same aspect (leading to infinite regress or attainment of the attained) or it is not (making recognition impossible). The response clarifies that the term 'indication' (dalālah) here is used loosely to mean 'leading to' (īṣāl), not in its technical sense. The true relation is between reasoning and its result, not a static quality of the evidence. The commentator then explains that the crucial point is the 'aspect' (ḥaythiyyah): the knowledge sought is assent knowledge of the indicated as indicated. If that depended on knowledge of the indication (itself a relation), circularity would follow because knowledge of a relation requires knowledge of its relata. However, the response implicitly shows that the prior knowledge needed is conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur) of the terms, not the relation itself, thus avoiding the circle. The text breaks off, but the argument is that the required prior knowledge is not of the indication as a relation but of the terms, which is already available.

الدليل
المدلول
الحيثية
الإضافة
التسامح

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection shows an inescapable circularity that undermines all reasoning.
Correction: The response uses the concept of aspect (ḥaythiyyah) to show that only conceptual knowledge of the terms is needed, not knowledge of the indication as a relation, thus avoiding circularity.

Checkpoint
What does 'tolerance' (tasāmuḥ) mean in the context of the response to the sixth objection?
A) That the commentator permits multiple interpretations of 'indication'.
B) That the term 'indication' is used loosely to mean 'leading to', not in its technical sense.
C) That the objection is tolerated despite its flaws.
D) That the evidence and its indication are treated as identical.

Key Takeaway
The sixth objection is deflected by recognizing that 'indication' is used loosely to mean 'leading to', and the prior knowledge required is merely conceptual, not relational, thus avoiding circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an objection that seems to attack the very foundation of your belief, do you immediately assume it is unanswerable? How does this lesson's careful analysis of 'aspect' challenge you to examine objections more precisely before accepting their conclusions?
?
Consider a time you dismissed a rational proof for a truth of your faith because you thought it presupposed what it was trying to prove. Did you ever stop to ask whether the prior knowledge required was merely conceptual—already present in your mind? What would it mean for you to trust that God has already given you the concepts needed to understand His proofs?
?

Distinguishing Indication from Aspect of Indication
Sanusi responds to circularity by distinguishing indication from its aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distinguishing between indication (dalala) and the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) resolves the objection of circularity in reasoning.

Core Idea
The aspect of indication, not the indication itself, makes reasoning beneficial.

The objection of circularity threatens the very possibility of theological reasoning. If knowing a proof's indication depends on already knowing the signified, then reasoning seems impossible. Sanusi responds by making a crucial distinction between the indication itself and the aspect of indication. This short passage lays the foundation for a proper understanding of how proofs function.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a response.
The objector claims that knowledge of the indication depends on knowledge of the signified, leading to circularity.
2
Identify the distinction
Focus on the response: 'قلنا : لا تتوقف إفادة النظر على العلم بالدلالة بل على العلم بوجه دلالته'. The reasoning's benefit depends on knowledge of the aspect of indication, not the indication itself.
The aspect of indication is a property in the proof that causes the mind to transfer to the signified.
3
Reflect on why it works
Consider how this distinction breaks the cycle. The aspect of indication is known prior to reasoning, while the indication (being a proof that leads to knowledge) only arises after reasoning.
Thus, there is no vicious circle.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لزم الدور) لأن العلم بدلالة الدليل على المدلول يتوقف على العلم بالمدلول ضرورة أن العلم بالإضافة مسبوق بالعلم بالمضافين، فيتوقف كل واحد من العلم بالمدلول، وإفادة النظر إياه على الآخر ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تتوقف إفادة النظر على العلم بالدلالة لزم كون الدليل دليلاً وكون النظر فيه مفيداً للعلم بالمدلول، (وإن لم يعتبر) ولم يعلم (وجه دلالته عليه ) وأنه باطل ( لأن الدليل إذا لم يعتبر وجه دلالته على المدلول كان أجنبياً منقطع التعلق عنه فلا يكون النظر فيه مفيداً للعلم به (قلنا : لا تتوقف ) يفيده النظر العلم التصديقي به فلا دور وقيل : الظاهر أن مبنى لزوم الدور هو أن العلم بالشيء تحققه لأن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه، فيتوقف العلم بالدلالة على نفس الدلالة، فيدور وليس بشيء لأن معنى كون العلم ظلا لمعلومه أنه حكاية عنه وأن المطابقة تعتبر جانبه سواء كان متقدماً على المعلوم أو متأخراً عنه، حتى لو انتفى المطابقة بينهما لم يكن علماً بل جهلاً، وليس معناه أنه فرع لوقوعه وإلا لزم انتفاء العلم الفعلي ولم يكن الواجب من العلم عالماً بالأشياء قبل وقوعها.

قوله : ( وإن لم يعتبر ولم يعلم وجه (دلالته مبناه إما عدم الفرق بين وجه الدلالة والدلالة كما يدل عليه التعرض لبيان الفرق بينهما في الجواب، وإما أن وجه الدلالة إنما يعتبر للعلم بالدلالة فإذا لم تتوقف الدلالة على العلم بها لم يكن لاعتبار وجه الدلالة وجه، فالتعرض لبيان الفرق فائدة زائدة على الجواب .

قوله : ( ووجه الدلالة إلخ) مقدمة ثانية للجواب على التقرير الأول، وكلام مبتدأ على التقرير الثاني لتمام الجواب بدونه كما علمت .
قوله : ( وإفادته إلخ) أي بعد إفادته قد عرفت أن الدلالة غير الإفادة، وأن الأول مسب الثاني ومن لم يفهم الفرق وقع لبيان البعدية في حيص بيص.
قوله : (بعد النظر فيه وإفادته فإن قلت : كونه هو عين إفادته كما يشعر به تفسير الشارح في مفتتح الشبهة فكيف يتأخر عنها؟ قلت: هو من قبيل قولهم : كون زيد عالماً يتوقف على علمه فليتدبر.
Circularity follows because knowledge of the indication (dalala) of the proof to the signified depends on knowledge of the signified, necessarily because knowledge of a relation (idafa) is preceded by knowledge of the two relata, so each of knowledge of the signified and the reasoning's benefit (ifada) depends on the other. (Otherwise) i.e., if the reasoning's benefit did not depend on knowledge of the indication, then the proof would be a proof and reasoning in it would be beneficial for knowledge of the signified, (even if one does not consider) and does not know (the aspect of its indication (wajh dalalatihi) upon it) and that is false because if the aspect of its indication upon the signified is not considered, it would be alien and disconnected from it, so reasoning in it would not be beneficial for knowledge of it. (We said: it does not depend) reasoning in the proof benefits assertive knowledge (al-ilm al-tasdiqi) of the signified, so there is no circularity. It was said: apparently the basis of the necessary circularity is that knowledge of a thing is a branch of its realization because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself, so knowledge of the indication depends on the indication itself, thus circularity. But this is nothing because the meaning of knowledge being a shadow of the known is that it is a representation of it and that correspondence is considered on its side whether it precedes the known or follows it, to the point that if correspondence is absent between them it would not be knowledge but ignorance. And its meaning is not that it is a branch of its occurrence, otherwise actual knowledge would be negated and the Necessary (al-Wajib) would not be knowing of things before their occurrence. His saying: 'so each one etc.' The dependence of the reasoning's benefit on knowledge of the signified is apparent from what preceded. As for the dependence of knowledge of the signified on the reasoning's benefit, no, unless it is said that knowledge of the theoretical signified depends on reasoning in reality. But in it, that the known implies the theoretical, it does not depend on it. So better to say: knowledge of the signified precedes the reasoning's benefit that precedes it, so circularity follows, i.e., the precedence of a thing over itself which is its necessary consequence. His saying: 'and that reasoning in it etc.' is an explicative conjunction based on the tolerance he committed in interpreting indication. His saying: 'and if one does not consider and does not know the aspect of its indication' its basis is either no difference between the aspect of indication and indication, as indicated by attempting to show the difference between them in the answer, or that the aspect of indication is only considered for knowledge of the indication, so if indication does not depend on knowledge of it, there is no point in considering the aspect of indication. So attempting to show the difference is an extra benefit beyond the answer. His saying: 'circularity follows' it was said: this aspect is also refuted by benefit of conjecture. Then apparently the basis of circularity is that knowledge of a thing is a branch of its realization because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself, as he stated before, in repelling the argument of those who say that what is belief is necessary for the legally responsible person, so it is necessary. So knowledge of the indication then depends on the indication itself, thus circularity. But as for what the commentator mentioned, in it obvious discussion because the assent (tasdiq) to the signified depends on benefit, and it depends on assent to the indication which depends on conceptualization of the signified, because knowledge of a relation is preceded by conceptualization of the two relata, not assent to them, so no circularity. And it may be answered that assent to the indication depends on assent to the signified also, because the relation is implied by the two relata, and assent to the existence of the implied requires assent to its necessary concomitant. But in it that the necessary concomitant known is that assent to the existence of the implied requires assent to the existence of its necessary concomitant after knowledge of the entailment, not dependence on it, so ponder. His saying: 'but depends on knowledge etc.' and the aspect of indication is other than indication, so it does not follow from not considering knowledge of it not considering knowledge of it (the indication). Or it is said: knowledge of the aspect of indication is only for the dependence of indication and benefit on it, not for knowledge of benefit such that from not considering this follows not considering that. His saying: 'and the aspect of indication etc.' is a second premise for the answer on the first formulation, and an independent statement on the second formulation for completeness of the answer without it as you know. His saying: 'and its benefit etc.' i.e., after its benefit, you have known that indication is other than benefit, and that the first is a cause of the second, and whoever does not understand the difference falls into confusion about explaining posteriority. His saying: 'after reasoning in it and its benefit, if you say: it is identical to its benefit as suggested by the commentator's interpretation at the beginning of the objection, so how is it posterior to it? I say: it is of the type of their saying: Zaid being knowledgeable depends on his knowledge, so ponder.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection of circularity (al-dawr) in the context of reasoning (al-nazar). The objector argues that if knowledge of a proof's indication (dalala) to its signified (madlul) depends on knowledge of the signified, and the reasoning's benefit (ifada) depends on knowledge of the indication, then there is a vicious circle. The author responds by distinguishing between the indication itself and the 'aspect of indication' (wajh al-dalala). The reasoning's benefit is said to depend on knowledge of the aspect of indication, not on the indication per se. The aspect of indication is defined as the property in the proof that causes the mind to transfer from the proof to the signified, and it exists in the proof whether or not someone engages in reasoning. Thus, no circularity occurs because the aspect of indication is known prior to reasoning, while the actual indication (being a proof that leads to knowledge) only arises after reasoning. The passage then critiques a view that knowledge is a 'shadow' of the known and argues that this does not require that knowledge is dependent on the actual occurrence of the known. The commentary then elaborates on various points, including a better formulation of the circularity (as the precedence of a thing over itself) and the distinction between conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Key Terms
الدور (al-dawr)
الدلالة (al-dalala)
وجه الدلالة (wajh al-dalala)
الإفادة (al-ifada)
النظر (al-nazar)
التصديق (al-tasdiq)
التصور (al-tasawwur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conflating indication (dalala) with the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) leads to the mistaken inference of circularity.
Correction: The aspect of indication is a distinct property in the proof; reasoning's benefit depends on it, not on the indication itself.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the circularity objection, what must be known for reasoning to produce knowledge of the signified?
A) The indication (dalala) itself
B) The aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala)
C) The signified (madlul) before reasoning
D) The assent of the reasoning person

Key Takeaway
Reasoning's benefit depends on the aspect of indication, not the indication itself, preventing circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present an argument for a belief you hold, do you focus more on convincing others of the indication (that it is true) or on helping them see the aspect that makes it compelling? Which approach is more faithful to the truth?
?
Have you ever accepted a proof without understanding the aspect that makes it a proof? How did that affect your certainty? Are there beliefs you hold today that rest on such shallow foundations?
?
Sanusi’s distinction protects reasoning from circularity. Are there areas in your life where your reasoning goes in circles because you haven’t identified the aspect of indication? What step can you take today to break that cycle?

Refuting the 'Shadow of the Known' Objection
Sanusi refutes the view that knowledge is ontologically dependent on the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that knowledge is a 'shadow' of the known does not entail circularity in reasoning.

Core Idea
Knowledge being a shadow of the known means correspondence, not ontological dependence.

Having resolved the initial circularity objection, Sanusi addresses a subtler challenge: the claim that knowledge is a branch of the known, making knowledge of indication dependent on the indication itself. This would reintroduce circularity. The response clarifies what it means for knowledge to be a 'shadow' of its object, preserving the integrity of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage where the objector claims that knowledge is a shadow of the known, thus knowledge of indication depends on the indication's existence.
This would create a new circularity: knowledge of indication requires the indication to exist, but the indication may itself depend on reasoning.
2
Analyze the response
Sanusi explains that the 'shadow' means a representation (hikaya) and correspondence (mutabaqa), not ontological derivation. Knowledge can precede its object.
The example of God's knowledge of things before their creation shows that knowledge does not depend on the known's existence.
3
Apply the distinction
Reflect on how this response reinforces the earlier distinction between dalala and wajh al-dalala.
The aspect of indication is known before reasoning, and knowledge of it is a correspondence to an existing property in the proof.

قوله : ( لزم الدور) قيل : هذا الوجه أيضاً منقوض بإفادة الظن هذا، ثم الظاهر أن مبنى لزوم الدور هو أن العلم بالشيء فرع تحققه لأن العلم بوقوع شيء ظل لوقوعه في نفسه على ما صرح به من قبل، في دفع احتجاج القائلين بأن ما اعتقاده لازم للمكلف ،ضروري فيتوقف العلم بالدلالة حينئذ على نفس الدلالة فيدور، وأما ما ذكره الشارح ففيه بحث ظاهر لأن التصديق بالمدلول موقوف على الإفادة وهي تتوقف على التصديق بالدلالة المتوقف على تصور المدلول، لأن العلم بالإضافة مسبوق بتصور المضافين لا التصديق بهما فلا دور وقد يجاب بأن التصديق بالدلالة متوقف على التصديق بالمدلول أيضاً، لأن الإضافة ملزوم للمضافين والتصديق بوجود الملزوم ملزوم للتصديق بلازمه وفيه أن اللازم المعلوم استلزام التصديق بوجود الملزوم التصديق بوجود لازمه بعد العلم بالملازمة لا توقفه عليه فتدبر.

قوله : ( ووجه الدلالة إلخ) مقدمة ثانية للجواب على التقرير الأول، وكلام مبتدأ على التقرير الثاني لتمام الجواب بدونه كما علمت .
قوله : ( وإفادته إلخ) أي بعد إفادته قد عرفت أن الدلالة غير الإفادة، وأن الأول مسب الثاني ومن لم يفهم الفرق وقع لبيان البعدية في حيص بيص.
قوله : (بعد النظر فيه وإفادته فإن قلت : كونه هو عين إفادته كما يشعر به تفسير الشارح في مفتتح الشبهة فكيف يتأخر عنها؟ قلت: هو من قبيل قولهم : كون زيد عالماً يتوقف على علمه فليتدبر.
Circularity follows because knowledge of the indication (dalala) of the proof to the signified depends on knowledge of the signified, necessarily because knowledge of a relation (idafa) is preceded by knowledge of the two relata, so each of knowledge of the signified and the reasoning's benefit (ifada) depends on the other. (Otherwise) i.e., if the reasoning's benefit did not depend on knowledge of the indication, then the proof would be a proof and reasoning in it would be beneficial for knowledge of the signified, (even if one does not consider) and does not know (the aspect of its indication (wajh dalalatihi) upon it) and that is false because if the aspect of its indication upon the signified is not considered, it would be alien and disconnected from it, so reasoning in it would not be beneficial for knowledge of it. (We said: it does not depend) reasoning in the proof benefits assertive knowledge (al-ilm al-tasdiqi) of the signified, so there is no circularity. It was said: apparently the basis of the necessary circularity is that knowledge of a thing is a branch of its realization because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself, so knowledge of the indication depends on the indication itself, thus circularity. But this is nothing because the meaning of knowledge being a shadow of the known is that it is a representation of it and that correspondence is considered on its side whether it precedes the known or follows it, to the point that if correspondence is absent between them it would not be knowledge but ignorance. And its meaning is not that it is a branch of its occurrence, otherwise actual knowledge would be negated and the Necessary (al-Wajib) would not be knowing of things before their occurrence. His saying: 'so each one etc.' The dependence of the reasoning's benefit on knowledge of the signified is apparent from what preceded. As for the dependence of knowledge of the signified on the reasoning's benefit, no, unless it is said that knowledge of the theoretical signified depends on reasoning in reality. But in it, that the known implies the theoretical, it does not depend on it. So better to say: knowledge of the signified precedes the reasoning's benefit that precedes it, so circularity follows, i.e., the precedence of a thing over itself which is its necessary consequence. His saying: 'and that reasoning in it etc.' is an explicative conjunction based on the tolerance he committed in interpreting indication. His saying: 'and if one does not consider and does not know the aspect of its indication' its basis is either no difference between the aspect of indication and indication, as indicated by attempting to show the difference between them in the answer, or that the aspect of indication is only considered for knowledge of the indication, so if indication does not depend on knowledge of it, there is no point in considering the aspect of indication. So attempting to show the difference is an extra benefit beyond the answer. His saying: 'circularity follows' it was said: this aspect is also refuted by benefit of conjecture. Then apparently the basis of circularity is that knowledge of a thing is a branch of its realization because knowledge of the occurrence of something is a shadow of its occurrence in itself, as he stated before, in repelling the argument of those who say that what is belief is necessary for the legally responsible person, so it is necessary. So knowledge of the indication then depends on the indication itself, thus circularity. But as for what the commentator mentioned, in it obvious discussion because the assent (tasdiq) to the signified depends on benefit, and it depends on assent to the indication which depends on conceptualization of the signified, because knowledge of a relation is preceded by conceptualization of the two relata, not assent to them, so no circularity. And it may be answered that assent to the indication depends on assent to the signified also, because the relation is implied by the two relata, and assent to the existence of the implied requires assent to its necessary concomitant. But in it that the necessary concomitant known is that assent to the existence of the implied requires assent to the existence of its necessary concomitant after knowledge of the entailment, not dependence on it, so ponder. His saying: 'but depends on knowledge etc.' and the aspect of indication is other than indication, so it does not follow from not considering knowledge of it not considering knowledge of it (the indication). Or it is said: knowledge of the aspect of indication is only for the dependence of indication and benefit on it, not for knowledge of benefit such that from not considering this follows not considering that. His saying: 'and the aspect of indication etc.' is a second premise for the answer on the first formulation, and an independent statement on the second formulation for completeness of the answer without it as you know. His saying: 'and its benefit etc.' i.e., after its benefit, you have known that indication is other than benefit, and that the first is a cause of the second, and whoever does not understand the difference falls into confusion about explaining posteriority. His saying: 'after reasoning in it and its benefit, if you say: it is identical to its benefit as suggested by the commentator's interpretation at the beginning of the objection, so how is it posterior to it? I say: it is of the type of their saying: Zaid being knowledgeable depends on his knowledge, so ponder.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection of circularity (al-dawr) in the context of reasoning (al-nazar). The objector argues that if knowledge of a proof's indication (dalala) to its signified (madlul) depends on knowledge of the signified, and the reasoning's benefit (ifada) depends on knowledge of the indication, then there is a vicious circle. The author responds by distinguishing between the indication itself and the 'aspect of indication' (wajh al-dalala). The reasoning's benefit is said to depend on knowledge of the aspect of indication, not on the indication per se. The aspect of indication is defined as the property in the proof that causes the mind to transfer from the proof to the signified, and it exists in the proof whether or not someone engages in reasoning. Thus, no circularity occurs because the aspect of indication is known prior to reasoning, while the actual indication (being a proof that leads to knowledge) only arises after reasoning. The passage then critiques a view that knowledge is a 'shadow' of the known and argues that this does not require that knowledge is dependent on the actual occurrence of the known. The commentary then elaborates on various points, including a better formulation of the circularity (as the precedence of a thing over itself) and the distinction between conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Key Terms
الدور (al-dawr)
الدلالة (al-dalala)
وجه الدلالة (wajh al-dalala)
الإفادة (al-ifada)
النظر (al-nazar)
التصديق (al-tasdiq)
التصور (al-tasawwur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge being a 'shadow' of the known means it depends ontologically on the known's existence.
Correction: It means correspondence (mutabaqa), not ontological dependence; knowledge can precede the known.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the 'shadow' objection, what does it mean for knowledge to be a shadow of the known?
A) Knowledge is a branch of the known's existence
B) Knowledge is a representation that corresponds to the known
C) Knowledge must come after the known in time
D) Knowledge is identical to the known

Key Takeaway
Knowledge's being a shadow of the known means it corresponds to it, not that it depends on its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your knowledge of something is only valid after you see it happen? How does this lesson challenge your reliance on sensory experience alone? This week, identify one truth you accept by rational proof rather than direct observation and reflect on why it is still certain.
?
Do you truly believe that God knew every detail of your life before you were born? If so, how does that shape your trust in His wisdom when you face unexpected trials? Ponder a specific difficulty you are facing now: do you live as if God's prior knowledge of it means He is aware and in control?
?
The lesson distinguishes between knowledge as representation (valid before existence) and knowledge as branch (invalid). In your own learning, do you sometimes treat understanding as dependent on tangible proof? Name one area in your life where you need to accept knowledge based on correspondence to truth rather than personal experience.

The Seventh Objection: Necessity of Knowledge and Taklif
Objection-and-response: trilemma and resolution via custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seventh objection against the necessity of knowledge is refuted by distinguishing rational necessity from customary occurrence.

Core Idea
Knowledge from reasoning is customarily produced, not rationally forced.

The seventh objection argues that if knowledge is necessary, then taklif is meaningless. The response clarifies that the necessity here is customary, not rational, which preserves the validity of taklif. In this passage, the commentator unpacks the opponents' trilemma and shows that their own premises lead to the same conclusion as the author's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage to find the core claim: if knowledge from reasoning is necessary and unavoidable, then obligating it is absurd.
The objection is labeled the 'seventh objection' and focuses on the impossibility of taklif for necessary knowledge.
2
Analyze the trilemma
Notice how the response breaks down the opponents' assumptions into three cases: necessary knowledge, theoretical knowledge with necessary attainment, and theoretical knowledge without necessary attainment.
Each case is shown to lead to a problem for the opponents' own position.
3
Understand the resolution
See how the opponents' concession that knowledge follows customarily aligns with the author's view: reasoning produces knowledge as a custom, not by rational necessity.
This distinction resolves the objection and shows the opponents' argument backfires.
